FLINT WILL PROBABLY WIN FIGHT FOR TOGA
SACRAMENTO. Jan. 10.âGeorge Hatton, Senator George C. Perkins' political director, arrived here to-night on the 7:30 o'clock train and went quietly to apartments that had been engaged
for him. Late this evening he was called into a caucus ef Fisk's forces. For some time the situation was discussed and then the agreement was reached that to-morrow morning a majority of
Fisk's supporters, at least, wilj enter the Flint caucus and will probably carry others with them. At a late hour to-night Walter Parker, Flint's most active lieutenant, said that he had secured
more than enough names to the caucus call to insure Flint's election on the first ballot to-morrow, and that at noon Frank P. Flint would be declared the legally elected United States Senator to
succeed Thomas R. Bard, the incumbent.

Candidate in Hiding While

r. Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 10.âThe Flint
tnen were elated when their candidate,
true to the assertion of the Reuf dele
gation, developed the strength of 47
votes on the first ballot. They were
busy as bees about the Golden Eagle
lobby and in their candidate's head
quarters, congratulating each other on
the result of the ballot and the victory
that appeared to them to be at hand.
"There was a scramble of politicians
who had been teetering in the fight to
get next to the man who seemed about
to step into Bard's place. The hands
of the Flint men were wrung until they
ached and the elevator boy shed honest
perspiration as he worked the rope in
. carrying Flint's permanent friends and
his newly developed admirers up and
down the shaft.
CANDIDATE DISAPPEARS.
Candidate Flint escaped the rush and
many a glad handshake intended for
him never landed. Not long after the
.Assembly had adjourned he disap
peared. Late in the afternoon it was
announced at his headquarters that he
wa6 indisposed and had retired to his
private apartments. Early in the night
It was given out that he had gone to
bed. Except for some of the Los An
geles boosters and the polite colored
man who Is ever on duty there, the
â > headquarters were deserted long before
midnight.
Whether Candidate Flint had gone to
bed or whether he was In consultation
\vith Ruef and his other supporters is
not known. He was, at any rate, not
et his headquarters during the night.
John Mott of Los Angeles, one of the
men who has stayed close to Flint dur
ing most of the time he has been here,
was the only person whp seemed au
thorized to make a statement for Flint,
and all he would venture was that the
"Senatorial aspirant had gone to bed.
AGAIN GO TO WORK.
With the first exultation over, the
practical Flint men settled down to
work again. Having been in the game
before, they knew that a man is not
elected when he is fourteen shy of the
right number. In fact, the fourteen
made therifall the more nervous. They
â¢ began to evince a desire to shut out
competition, to close the affair up while
the goal was in reach, and before any
rival could bring about a situation that
(would be dangerous.
Work was begun at once on every
anti-Flint man whose adhesion to the
candidate for whom he had cast his
vote was not strong. There were some
who had not voted for Flint who were
really for him, and some who were
ready to jump any way the victory
seems certain.
Flint workers of the practical sort
were untiring during the afternoon and
toward evening a movement to secure
a caucus of 60 and declare Flint its
nominee was begun. Senator Hahn
circulated a roll call and It began to
fill rapidly with the men who had voted
for Flint, but 60 names were not ob
tained. The most the Flint supporters
claimed as agreeing to go into caucus
was 62, an alleged gain of 5 for the
Los Angeles man through the prose
lyting and the accession of any who
had determined to go over to Flint or
the leader after the first ballot. The
Claim of the Flint people is that 47
were actually present at the caucus.
Friends of the other candidates claimed
that the total number of on the
call was only 47 and but 31 were ac
tually present at the caucus.
GATHER FOR CAUCUS.
. The gathering lasted but a few min
.utes and was adjourned on the state
ment that it was desired to learn the
results of the caucuses then being held
by the supporters of other candidates.
The caucus was first called for 8
o'clock, then for 8:30, then for 9. To
ward the latter hour there were not
more than twenty legislators In the
Assembly chamber and some of these
were suporters of candidates other
than Flint, who had remained up to
the hour of the caucus to finish work.
Senator Hahn railed the gathering to
order. Assemblyman Bliss acted as
chairman and Assemblyman Houser as
secretary. Senator Hahn announced
from the floor that the eighteen men
then in caucus wanted the larger one
to go over until 9 o'clock the next
morning. The assembled legislators
then adjourned to the hour named and
the hotel lobbies were soon full of Flint
voters again.
The Flint men claimed to-night that
six of the Fisk votes had come over to
their candidate. They would give no
names. Some claims were made by
men not authorized to speak for leg
islators who, upon being interviewed,
declared they did not intend to change
their votes. It is pretty certain that
these six will change to Flint to-mor
row: Welsh, Amerige, Held, Jarvis,
McQowan and Pfaeffle. McGowan will
change on the second ballot and all the
others on the first. The following also
will change to Flint, bo some of his

RUEF AND STONE AID.
At the caucus of the Ruef men this
morning it took but a short time to
give the word for Flint. When the dele
gation got down to the sidewalk Can
didate Flint was there and he was soon
surrounded by the bunch, each of whom
received a handshake from the man
they hoped to make Senator.
One of the secrets revealed since to
day's ballot is that General George
Stone has been working all the time
for Flint. The general Is happy over
the situation and it is said that in case
of Flint's victory he will ba, one of the
first men to be rewarded.
"Stand Pat" is the slogan of George
A. Knight. He says that he can see
no signs of victory for Flint and that
he Is going to remain in till the decid
ing ballot is cast. Knight said further
that he had gathered his forces to
gether and that he had absolute assur
ances that there were no deflections
from his camp, though Flint's lieuten
ants show the signatures on their cau
cus papers of a few of Knight's men.
Judge Dibble, who is managing Knight's
fight, said he had seen harder battles
won, and they have got to show him
more than they have before he will
hoist the white flag and capitulate.
SPECULATION ON FISK.
"Stand pat" Is also the watchword of
Arthur G. Fisk's forces. Fisk was
busy rounding up his forces to-night
and addressing them on the situation
at two separate meetings. He was in
formed that Flint's men claimed they
had taken from him some of the pillars
of support, but he would have none of
it. He claimed positively that his forces
would standby him until he was elect
ed or released them from their obliga
tion. Fisk's forces will meet again at
8 o'clock in the morning.
"You know I can elect Flint at a
word." he said. "But while this is true
I am not going to do it. We are going
to stand pat."
Withal the situation here to-night is
most interesting. Flint's confidence
does not outshine the confidence of the
supporters of Bard, Knight and Fisk
in their contention that he has a hard
er road yet to travel than he imagines. I
Oxnard's letter arrived at a time and
was worded in a manner that caused
much speculation as to just what his
next move would be, every man here
being aware of his skill as a manipu
lator âskill that proved successful in
Washington in the game of politics
played with men of much greater ex
perience than those that compose the
California Legislature.
Fisk's position is attracting the most
attention, however. While it is admit
ted that a majority of his followers are
standing by him through motives of
personal friendship, it does not follow
that they will move into any other
camp at his bidding. Though Fisk re
marked that he could elect Flint at any
minute he desired, If such desire should
seize him, there are-some that disagree
with him, saying that his following will

OXNARD HOLDS ALOOF.
Sends Letter Explaining His Attitude
on the Fight.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 10.âThis
morning Henry T. Oxnard decided
that his name should not go before
the Legislature until after the first
or second ballot. Accordingly he pre
pared a letter, a copy of which he de
livered bo\h to the Flint and Bard
managers. The text o*. Oxnard's let
ter follows:
"Sacramento, Cal., Jan. 10.
"Hon. Frank P. Flint Hon. Chester
A. Rowell, Hon. D. T. Perkins, F. J.
Brandon, Otto GerberdingâDear sirs:
I have many friends among the mem
bers of the Legislature who have ex
pressed an intention of voting for me,
but, being interested in securing the
Senatorship for Southern California
and in pursuance of the policy under
which I have been acting for some
time, my name will not be formally
presented to the Legislature to-day,
nor my friends be asked to vote for
me until after a ballot or two has been
taken.
"I have arrived at this conclusion
through fear that a multiplicity of
candidates from Southern California,
in the preliminary balloting, might
endanger its interests.
"However, should neither of the
leading candidates secure the support
necessary to elect I shall expect my
friends in the Legislature to be given
an opportunity to exercise their pref
erence for me. Yours very truly,
"HENRY T. OXNARD."
AMERICAN SCHOONER FOUND
STRANDED AND DESERTED
The Mary Lee Patton, From Philadel
phia. Located at the Bahama
Islands.
NASSAU, N. P.. Jan. 10. â The
American schooner Mary Lee Patton,
from Philadelphia December 2 for
Cardenas, has been found stranded
and deserted at Abaco, Bahama Isl
ands.
The Mary Lee Patton was built in
Baltimore in 1894 and is 522 tons net.
She is owned by her captain, Lewis
S. Steelman of Bridgport. Conn.

SACRAMENTO, Jan. 10.âAll the
camps caucused to-night and more cau
cusing will take place to-morrow. At
the Bard camp, 19 of the 22 attended
the roundup. Assemblyman Perkins
and Frank Brandon assert that the
Bard forces will not break. Stories to
the effect that Amerige of Orange has
gone over to Flint are denied. It was
declared at the Bard headquarters that
Amerige would stand as firm and as
long as Anderson, and the latter is
rated as a Bard stalwart who will
fall, if he falls at all. on the firing line.
The story that Senator Oreenwell is
wavering is also discredited.
Otto Gerberding, Bard's chief man
ager upon the outside, was called to
Livermore to-day on account of the
illness of his sister. He wired to-night
advising the Bard men to stand firm,
as he had received assurances of ac
cessions to the Bard column. Thomas
Hughes, R. N. Bulla, C. T. Elliott.
James A. Louttitt, C. A. Young and
others of the Bard management Insist
that the fight Is still open and that all
Flint's abilities Will be required to hold
the strength that he developed to-day.
"It is not true," said one Bard man,
"that 52 or even 47 legislators attended
the Flint caucus. The actual attend
ance was 30, and the number of names
signed to the caucus tallied exactly
with his vote for Senator to-day", 47,
and no more."
The Fisk men held a prolonged con
ference to-night. Fisk said: "Only six
men were absent from Ihe caucus, and
I have seen them since the conference.
I am assured that there will be no
break to-morrow. The Senatorial
contest is not settled."
Senator Woodward of Sonoma laughs
at the rumor that he is going over to
the Flint camp upon the assurance that
he will be appointed superintendent of
the San Francisco Mint as soon as
Senator Flint has arrived in Washing
ton.
Henry T. Oxnard appeared for a few
minutes tc*-night in the lobby of the
Golden Eagle looking as debonair as
usual, but he did not mix with the
politicians gathered there. .
The Knight men caucused to-ntght.
Knight flatly contradicted the report
that Senator Rush of Solano is going
over to join the forces of the Los
Angeles candidate.
Ruef received a message to-night
concerning the condition of his father
which was so encouraging that he de
cided to remain here another day.

VIENNA, Jan. 10.âThe Zelt accuses

SEVERE SNOWSTORM
DESCENDS ON KANSAS
Wheat Benefited, but Livestock Will
Suffer âFlakes Fall in Okla
homa,
TOPEKA, Kans., Jan. 10.âA heavy
snow fell throughout Kansas to-day.
In the Central Kansas wheatfields the
snow was heavier than in five years.
The temperature is low to-night, and
there is suffering among livestock in
the western portion of the State.
Wheat Is greatly benefited.
GUTHRIE, Okla., Jan. 10.âWet
snow and sleet has been falling gen
erally over Oklahoma since early this
morning, and now lies on the ground
to a depth of from two to five inches.
There is no suffering, and winter
wheat will be greatly benefited.

ingly the French Minister has been

and arrange for carrying out tko pol

DEMOCRATS SHOW
GEORGE A. KNIGHT
IS POPULAR WITH
PEOPLE IN GALLERY
Cheers Greet Lawyer's Name
Rowell Opens the Battle by
Nominating Senator Bard.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 10.âWhatever
may be the outcome of the Senatorial
situation the fact remains that with
the great audience that gathered in
the Senate to-day George A. Knight
was the popular candidate. At the
mention of every other name the
plause was restricted, but when Sen
ator Selvage concluded his eloquent
appeal for support for George A.
Knight the audience threw aside its
conservatism and the Senate chamber
t resounded with the sound of clapping
hands and stamping feet.
But as to oratory Senator Charles
M. Shortridge led his colleagues. He
seconded the nomination of Arthur G.
Fisk and he threw his whole soul into
the task. He said he knew no north,
no south; that the destiny of the Re
publican party was all that awakened
.. his patriotism, but if geography was
to be the question then his argument
would be given weight, for in the elec
tion of Fisk neither north nor south
would be offended; the great center
of the State would receive what was
due it apd its great population and
wealth would be honorably and loyally
representedv
Many prominent people gathered in
the Senate to watch the opening
skirmish In the Senatorial battle of
ballots. Governor Pardee's family ar
rived early and among the Senatorial
candidates were Knight and Fisk, who
chose to observe the proceedings from
the height of the gallery. Dibble re
inforced Knight and was surrounded
by the leading supporters of other
candidates, Walter Parker being prom
inent among the Flint contingent.
NOMINATES BARD.
Promptly at noon a resolution call
ing for the election of a United States
Senator to succeed Thomas R. Bard
was read and this was followed by a
recital of the law under which the
Senate was about to proceed. This
formality concluded Senator Chester
.Rowell of J?'resno rose to place the in
cumbent, Senator Bard, In nomination.
Senator Rowell read his nomination
speech, which, in part, was as follows:
"Six years ago I presented the name
of Thomas R. Bard in this Senate
chamber as a candidate for United
States Senator. At the close of that
long and memorable contest, which
ended in his election, I promised for
him a faithful and intelligent atten
tion to duties, a statesmanlike judg
ment on national and international
questions, fidelity to every interest of
California, and courteous and generous
personal treatment of all Republicans,
regardless of factional affiliations. 1
predicted for him high standing among
his associates at Washington. My
promises and predictions have been
fully realized. Almost from the mo
ment of his advent in the Senate his
influence in both great and small af
fairs has been evident.
"Senator Bard has earned a re-elec
tion. His election would heal factional
differences. It would satisfy every
business interest of the State. It
would reinforce our delegation in Con
gress and strengthen the national ad
ministration at Washington in ail its
efforts for good government. It would
be creditable to the Republican party.
It would confer honor upon Senator
Bard and reflect honor upon the State.
His defeat will cast a shadow upon
Republican politics in this State that
will take a generation to efface.
"Successful or defeated, he will re
main the foremost citizen of California,
noble in character, strong in his adher
ence to absolute right and honored by
every high-minded citizen of Califor
nia.
"Mr. President, Senator Bard's friends
and the people of California await the
outcome of this contest with faith in
his ultimate vindication and election.
Again I present the name of Thomas
R. Bard."
PRAISE FOR SENATOR.
Senator John N. Anderson of Santa
Ana seconded Senator Bard's nomina
tion and spoke impressively. "It is fit
ting, that the voice of the south be
heard seconding the voice of the north,"
he said. "I know of no man better
qualified to represent California in the
Federal Congress than is Senator Bard,
and I hear the call from across the
Rockies asking that he be returned.
It will be a shameful disregard of the
voice of the people if he is not sent
b.ack to complete the labors he has so
well begun, and I appeal to all of you
loyal Californians to lay aside your sec
tional prejudices and vote for the man
that brought harmony and will pre
serve it in the party of this State; and,
not only that, but I ask you to vote for
the man that served California honor
ably, against whose work no man can
complain and who in all justice and
right should be encouraged by the ap
proval of this body he so richly de
serves."
Senators Belshaw and Broughton also
seconded the nomination of Senator
Bard, paying tribute to his sterling
qualities and honorable record in Con
gress.
SELVAGE'S SPEECH.
George A. Knight was next placed in
nomination by Senator Selvage, who
said, in part:
â¢ "The most important duty imposed by
the constitution upon the Legislature
now confronts that body. We must
here elect a Senator who shall rep
resent our State in the Congress of the
United States for the next six years.
That we perform that duty well is de
manded of us alike by the people of
the State and of the nation. What we
do here in this relation may either re
flect credit upon us and be productive
of good, or it may bring upon us dis
credit and be productive of evil."
Senator Selvage went on to say that
the place, should go to a candidate
representing the northern portion of
the State, saying:
"In measuring the relative Impor
tance of the territory south and north
qt the Tehachapl, I find the south to be
about one-fifth In assessable property,
one-fourth in population and one
fourth in extent and about that rela
tion In Republicanism.
"George A. Knight is the man whc
can represent the people of California
in the halls of Congress better than
any other man who lives to-day on the
Pacific Coast. He possesses every
quality in a high degree to make him a
great representative of our people."
In his peroration Selvage paid a high
tribute to Knight's speech seconding
the nomination of Roosevelt for Presi
dent and in his closing sentence said:
"I therefore nominate for United
States Senator to represent this State
in Congress our most brilliant, most
popular and honored citizen of Cali
fornia, George A. Knight."
Senator John B. Irish was the first to
second Knight's nomination and was
followed by Senator Ralston, who made
an extended argument in support of his
contention that Knight should be re
warded for his long and efficient ser
vice in behalf of the Republican party.
HAHN FLINT'S SPONSOR.
Senator Benjamin W. Hahn of Pasa
dena placed Frank P. Flint in nomina
tion, his address being entirely matter
of-fact and dispassionate. In part he
said:
"The constitutional requirement of
representation is the embodiment of the
fundamental truth, that to properly
represent, one should properly know
what he represents. This is a crys
tallization of the idea that the man
should be intimately associated with
the people; conversant with and pos
sess as thorough a knowledge as pos
sible of ail the actual existing condi
, tlons that prevail in the locality that
he is chosen to represent.
"The great commonwealth of the
south has needs particularly its own.
Her fruit interests alone are clamoring
for an alert, vigorous and loyal pro
tector. From the stalwart ranks of her
citizenship she presents for your con
sideration a man, young in years,
strong and resourceful in his mental
equipment;broad in his sympathies, one
who represents in his great-hearted,
broad-minded development and domi
nant characteristic of the ideal suc
cessful man, that keynote of the high
est character and great abilityâthe
saving grace of common sense. He is
part and parcel of our State's growth
and development, having come here
when three years of age.

FISK IS NAMED.
Arthur G. Fisk was nominated for
Senator by Senator Wolfe of San Fran
ce i*c:o. Woife said in part:
"For the fourth time in my life
something that does not happen to
many menâl arise on the floor of the
Senate to participate in the election of
a gentleman to the, high and exalted
position of United States Senator from
the State of California. The eyes of the
world are on this State. We have
grown in population and our natural
resources are attracting the attention
of the world. I am not exaggerating
when I declare that the State of Cali
fornia is destined to be one of the
greatest States of the Union.
"It is therefore meet and proper that
this great State of ours should be rep
resented in the Senate of the United
States by a man of vigor, energy and
ability. He should, to my mind, be a
man of the people, who should know
their wishes and wants and be ever
ready to meet their demands. He
should be a man whose first duty and
first consideration will be representa
tion of the people, and who will be
ever ready to further the interests of
his constituency.
"I have in my mind the name of a
man who, in my humble judgment, will
meet all these requirements. Strong in
intellect, well equipped by nature, a
man of the plain people, who has spent
nearly his Whole life in this State, and
one who if elected to this high office
will be ever faithful to the peode of
this State, and yet will never forget
the men who helped to elevate him to
this exalted position. He will be close
to the administration. He will stand
by and back up the policy of President
Roosevelt, for to that foremost man of
this nation he is no stranger, having
already received recognition at his
hands which bears testimony to his
personal worth.
"I present for your consideration for
the high office of United States Senator
a man who once filled the position of
Speaker of the Assembly and who is
now the Postmaster of San Franciscoâ
the Hon. Arthur G. Fisk.'
Charles M. Shortridge and Henry W.
Lynch seconded Fisk's nomination,
both rising to the heights of eloquence
In the performance of the formality.
The most picturesque address of the
day was delivered by Senator J. B.
Sanford of Uklah, leader of the Demo
cratic minority, who nominated Con
gressman Theodore Bell of Napa for
Senator. E. J. Emmons of Bakersfleld
seconded the nomination of Bell and
concluded \he day's oratory. The vote
waÃ then called for.

SALOON TO BE LICENSED
IN UNIVERSITY TOWN
Verdict of Forest Grove Voters Likely
to Result In a Legal Fight.
FOREST GROVE, Ore., Jan. 10.â
The saloon license faction candidate
for Mayor won in the Forest Grove
election, after the hardest fought cam
paign in the city's history. The re
sult will be a licensed saloon for
Forest Groveâthe first It will ever
have had. A secondary probable re
sult will be a legal fight with the trus
tees of the Tualatin Academy and
Pacific University, who are expected
to bring suit to prevent the locating
of a saloon on lands once held in trust
fcr these institutions, a stipulation
being Inserted in all deeds granted
that no "ardent spirits" should be
sold on the premises.

EIGHT IN NUMBER,FOR CONGRESSMAN THEODORE BELL
ORATORICAL FLIGHTS
IN THE LOWER HOUSE
Sifver-Tonaued Bofons Praise Asp rants
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 10.-âFlint 31, ,
Bard 16, Knight 14, Fisk 14, Brooks
I, Bell 4.
That's the way the vote stood
when the tally was made up on the
first vote for United States Senator in
the Assembly to-day.
Never before in California has so
little interest in the election of a
United States Senator been shown in
the lower house. The gallery was not
filled and there was plenty of room
behind the rail and in the side aisles.
There was some applause for nomi
nating speeches and candidates'
names, Los Angeles people showing
the most enthusiasm, but the only
thing that approached a demonstration
was brought out by Assemblyman
Nathan Coghlan of San Francisco, who j
made the speech of the day in second- j
ing the nomination of George A. ,
Knight.
Flights of oratory from the young
men of the House were a feature of
the nominating work. Two more
stellbiders, Jones and Coghlan, were
aoded to the San Francisco list and
with McGowan and Anthony already
on the roll of ready debaters, with !
perhaps some talent yet to come for- ;
ward, it looks as if there was to be >
no lack of voice for the metropolis in ]
fhe lower house.
HOLSER'S ORATORY.
Houser of Los Angeles In making
the Flint nominating speech acquit- j
ted himself well and eloquently, ,
though he attempted a few empyrean .
flights that took away some of the ]
force of his really forceful speech and
gave, a slight suspicion of delsarte j
drilling before a looking glass. His j
address was far above the average
heard in the Assembly, though, and \
brought out much applause on its own
behalf. He said in part:
"In accordance with the requirements
of the law the Legislature is met to
day for the purpose of electing a Unit
ed States Senator. No higher duty de
volves upon us during our respective
legislative terms, and it should be with
a realization of a heavy responsibility
that we proceed to the performance of
our duty in this regard.
"Southern California, with her im
mense area, with her splendid length
of coast line, with her excellent harbors
and her varied industriesâ differing
vastly from those of the middle and
northern sections of the State, needs
and is entitled to represent her in the
United States Senate a man who is
fully alive to the situation.
"Such a man we have in the person
of Frank P. Flint of Los Angeles.
Broad of intellect, fined with noble
purpose, a man of sterling character
and unimpeachable integrity, he is a
profound student in the law and has an
accurate and scientific knowledge of
"In behalf of the district I represent,
in behalf of the vast majority of the
citizens of the peerless city of Los An
geles, in behalf of the imperial county
of Los Angeles, I have the honor to
present for your consideration as a
candidate for the high office of United
States Senator the name of Frank P.
Flint."
Dorsey, who is one of the brightest
of the young men in the House, also
acquitted himself admirably in sec*
onding Bard.
Much interest was manifested in the
vote of the Alameda delegation, as it
was supposed to give an idea of ad
ministration strength. Assemblyman
Waste's vote was awaited with es
pecial expectancy, for this reason. He
has at no time previous given an ex
pression of choice for Senator. When
"Pop" Bliss cheered Houser's speech,
the belief that he would vote for Flint
was made a certainty. Bates also ap
plauded the mention of Flint's name,
but he cast his vote for Fisk.
WASTE FOR FLINT.
Waste quietly replied "Flint" when
the clerk reached his name.
For some days there had been
doubt as to who would nominate Bard.
The choice fell upon Assemblyman
Perkins last night. His was the short
est speech made.
"I take pleasure in presenting the
name of an esteemed gentleman for the
high office of United States Senator. I
have known this gentleman for many
years and have been clo-sely identified
with him socially, as well as in a busi
ness way. , I now name the Hon. Thom
as R. Bard."

KNIGHT IS NAMED.
Assemblyman Kolley of Humboldt
in nominating Knight said:
"it affords me keen pleasure to
have the privilege and the honor of
placing in nomination for the most
exalted legislative position in the
world a gentleman who is the peer of
any man in California. I am more
than pleased that the opportunity is
afforded me to nominate for United
States Senator a citizen from Califor
nia who is known to the people from
one end of the State to the other. It
is our solemn duty to elect a man
who is broad enough to represent the
entire State of California; a man
who knows California and who is
known by her people from San Diego
to Del Norte, and from the Pacific to
the Nevada line. Have we such a
man? Yes. and the man is Theodore
Roosevelt's personal friend, George
A. Knight of California.
"Gentlemen of the Assembly, it is
my opinion that of all the men who
have been suggested for election as
United States Senator by this Legis
lature, cither through the press, by
the public speakers or by the private
citizens, George A. Knight is the
first and real choice of a great major
ity of the citizens of the State. George
A. Knight is a man known not only
to all of California, but throughout
this great country of ours.
"In campaign following campaign
for the last thirty years George A.
Knight's eloquent voice has been
heard from the party platform in
city, town and hamlet throughout
every section of this Immense State.
Without party reward or official rec
ognition for more than a quarter of
a century he has gladly given to the
Republican party his able services.
"We of California rejoiced that
whon the Hon. Theodore Roosevelt,
President of these United States, de
termined to select a man from the
Great Weft to second his nomination
at Chicago, George A. Knight was the
man of his choice.
"In conclusion I submit that from
fhe standpoint of personal ability,
from the standpoint of party ser
vices and from the standpoint of
obedience to the voice of the people of
California, we should elect George A.
Knight as United States Senator from
California."*

Assemblyman John nominated Ben

terests.

PORTLAND IMMIGRATION
OFFICERS DETAIN SHIPS
Inspector Charpcs Captains of For
eign Vessels With a Violation
of live Laws.
PORTLAND. Or., Jan. 10.âFor the
second time within a month a foreign
ship has been detained in this port
by the immigration inspector for vio
lation of the immigration laws.
In the first case the inspector re
fuses to issue necessary papers to en
able the master of a French craft to
obtain clearance papers on the ground
that a deserter from the ship wad
likely to become a public charge.
Captain James McDonald of the
British ship Hampton, which is ready
to sail, has been placed under arrest,
charged with having abandoned a
sailor while in this port.

Begin to take it TO-DAY.ADAMS TAKES
OATH OF OFFICE
Governor-Elect of Colorado
Is Inaugurated at Denver
Amid Booming of Cannon
ââ¢
PEABODY AT HIS SIDE
Retiring Executive Will Be
gin Contest for Seat as
Soon as He Can File Papers
DENVER. Jan. 10.âStanding beneath
the draped folds of the American flag,
while the walls about him and the
floor beneath him trembled in response
to cannon that roared a salute in his
honor, Alva Adams was, at noon to
day. Inaugurated as Governor of Colo
rado. While he stood with uplifted
hands, taking the oath of office admin
istered by Chief Justice Gabbert, there
stood at his side ex-Governor James H.
Peabody, who will to-morrow inaugur
ate the most energetic and bitter con
test that Colorado has ever seen for the
right to fill the position that Governor
Adams had just sworn by the ever
iiving God to administer to the best of
liis powers of body and mind.
The inauguration ceremonies were
simple- in the extreme. A few minutes
before 12 o'clock Governor-elect Adams
called at the office of Governor Pea
body, who waa to accompany him to
the House of Representatives, where
the inauguration was to be held. Arm
in arm, the two men, Peabody oir the
right, Adams on the left, entered the
House, which was packed almost to
suffocation by Senators, Representa
tives and visitors. Lieutenant Gover
nor Haggott, presiding over the Joint
session of the Legislature, greeted both
men aa they stepped upon the rostrum
with a shake of the hand, and seated
Governor Peabody on his right, the
Governor-elect on his left.
Chief Justice Gabbert stepped for
ward and in a few seconds the oath
was given and taken, and Alva Adams
was the Governor of Colorado.
Governor Adams theÂ«n commenced the
reading of his Inaugural address.
Ex-Governor Peabody's notice of
contest will be filed on January 13, the
last day allowed under the law. At
torney Henry J. Hersey, who is in
charge of the case, said this afternoon
that it would be difficult to prepare the
necessary papers before that time.

OREGON SENATE
ENDS DEADLOCK
William Kuykendall Elected
President of the Body on
the Sixty-Fourth Ballot
CARTER FORCES YIELD
After Period of Delay the
Legislators Will Now Be
Able to Take Up Business
SALEM. Ore., Jan. 10.âThe dead
lock in the Senate was broken to-day
by the election of Senator William
Kuykendall of Lane County to the
presidency.
When the higher branch of the Legis
lature met balloting was recommenced,
the deadlock remaining unbroken un
til the sixth-fourth ballot had been
taken. At this point signs of dis
ruption in the ranks of both Kuyken
dall's followers and his opponent's,
Senator E. V. Carter of Jackson, be
came apparent and a recess was taken
until 5 o'clock.
Carter immediately made overtures
to Kuykendall, which were accepted.
After several hours had been devoted
to an effort to induce the Carter fol
lowers to indorse the compromise, it
was announced that a caucus would
be held.
When the Senate reconvened at 5
o'clock Senator Carter, after thanking
his supporters, announced that he
withdrew from the race in favor of
Senator Kuykendall. .The sixty-fifth
ballot was then taken and the twenty
five Republican Senators all voted for
Kuykendall, he being declared elected.
The Senate then adjourned until to
morrow.
RAILWAY COMMISSION BILLS.
Two Measures Presented in the Wash
ington Legislature.
OLTMPIA, Wash., Jan. 10.âThe
two branches of the Legislature met
In joint session to-day and canvassed
the vote cast In the last general elec
tion for State officers.
Two railway commission bills were
Introduced in the Senate to-day. One
was very similar to the commission
bill of two years ago, which passed
the House, but was defeated in the
Senate; the other Is an ad
aptation of the Texas law and
is copied almost in detail. A
three-cent passenger rate bill affect
ing railroads was introduced also in
the Senate, as was a bill providing that
no railway man shall be employed
more than ten consecutive hours, ex
cept In case of great emergency, when
fourteen hours is the limit.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALIJ, WEDNESDAY, JANUARY 11, 1905.
TRAIN ORDERS
BY "WIRELESS"
|Two Eastern Railroads toi
Do Away With Present
System of Telegraphy
I FAR REACHING PROJECT'
Passengers May Receive Mes-!
sages While Traveling at
Rate of Fifty Miles an Hour
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO, Jan. 10.âArrangements
are being made for the installation of ,
wireless telegraphy on the New York
Central and Lake Shore railroads. It.
is proposed to equip the Twentieth
Century Limited and Lake Shore Lim- j
ited trains so that passengers on them
may send or receive messages at any
time, whether the train is standing still I
â or running at the rate of fifty miles j
an hour. Preliminary tests have satis- I
fled President Newman that this Is
possible with the wireless system of
telegraphy.
To provide communication with
trains in motion is only a part of the
New York Central's plans. Experi- i
ments have been made of the present
automatic block signals in combination!
with equipment for wireless telegraphy, |
whereby signals of danger have been
given in the engine cabin. This system j
of double warning to engineers will be .
I a most valuable safeguard against ac- j
j cidents and will make collisions almost'
j impossible.
Another object In installing the wire- ;
less system of telegraphy Is to be in
dependent of the present telegraph sys
tem. Storms which blow down tele
graph poles cause immense loss to rail
roads by delaying train service and In
crease the risk of accident.
If the tests made on the Lake Shore ,
and the New York Central prove sue- <
cessful, other railroads will adopt the I
wireless system.

PRESIDENT WITHDRAWS
LETTER TO A MAGAZINE
His Praise of Publication Used to
Promote Sales of Corpora
tion Stock.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 10.âPresident
Roosevelt to-day withdrew a letter
which he wrote last March extending
to the American Newsboys' Magazine
his wishes for its success. The with
drawal of the President's letter was
announced in the form of a letter
which the President wrote on the 7th
inst. to Murat Halstead, who was
president of the company which is to
publish the magazine. The letter of
the President to Mr. Halstead follows
in part:
Dear Mr. Halstead: I am advised that you
are the president of a company formed to pub
lish the American Newsboys' Magazine and
ccntemplate withdrawing your connection
with It.
After quoting in full the letter
which he wrote last March to Colonel
E. O. Stealey, then editor of the
magazine, the President continues:
As you will note this letter Is couched In
Keneral terms, but It has been used to exploit
this company. Certain facts have come to me
as to the character of certain persons inter
ested In the corporation, as well as the meth
ods they have adopted for the disposal of
stock, which make me unwilling longer to
permit the use of my name in connection with
the enterprise. May I ask you to look care
fully Into the matter and writ* ma your views?
I will not have any letter of mine used In
connection with any concern which Is in any
way tainted, even though this letter refers
more to a general movement than to any par
ticular scheme.
In response to this letter Halstead
wrote to the President that he intend
ed to withdraw from the company.
To-day the President, In addition to
authorizing the publication of the cor
respondence, took steps to insure the
withdrawal of his letter of March last.
W. R. HEARST ARGUES
FOR LETTER CARRIERS
Congressman Says That He Considers
President's Executive Order Arbi
trary and Restrictive.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 10. â The
House Committee on Reform In the
Civ'l Service to-day authorized a fav
orable report on the resolution Intro
duced by Representative Hearst catl
ing on the President for "all reports,
documents, papers and orders show
ing the executive action relating to
political activity by letter carriers
mentioned in his last annual message
and the grounds therefor;" also direct

nicate to the House all facts bearing
upon the dismissal of James C. Kel
ler, Frank Cunningham, Warren
Tumber and H. W. Aldrlch and the
grounds for their dismissal.
Hearst was heard by the committee.
He thought the President's executive
order prohibiting political activity
was ''arbitrary and restrictive." It
abridged not only the conduct of car
riers, he said, but also the conduct of
memters of Congress. Personally, he
liked to talk with the carriers from
his district and in his opinion the suc
cessful Congressman was the one who
met their demands properly.

SCORES PRESS
AND THEATER
Sacramento's Chief of Police
Says That the Sensational
Drama Demoralizes Youth
CRITICIZES NEWSPAPERS
Believes Too Much Promi
nence Is Given to Ac
counts of Criminal Acts
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO. Jan. 10âChief of
Police Martin Coffey, in his annual re
port to the City Board of Trustees, has
made several declarations which have
aroused no end of comment. Crime
among juveniles has been rampant here
of late and he ascribes this fact partly
to the presentation of such plays as
'â¢Raffles, the Amateur Cracksman," by
Kyrle Bellew. The chief declares all
such dramas should be denied a license
to play in Sacramento. He asserts that
Spencer Kendall, sent to the Prescott
School.of Industry for robbery, told
him he was an usher at the Clunie The
ater when "Raftles" was produced and
the witnessing of that play led him to
go out, buy a revolver and rob a drug
store.
Chief Coffey declares that the Tele
phone Exchange offices and department
stores are nothing more than kinder
gartens of vice for young girls, whose
poverty is daily thrown into contrast
with the apparent splendor of women
of questionable character who go into
the stores to buy. The chief asserts
that the telephone girls hear all man
ner of evil things over the line. He
adds that the scare headlines in a por
tion of the daily press should be for
bidden, and mentions two cases where,
he says, citizens killed Innocent men
whom they mistook for burglars while
excited over sensational accounts of
burglaries in the papers.
Colonel H. Weinstock, senior part
ner of the department store firm of
Weinstock, Lubin & Co., to-day said
that Chief Coffey had inflicted a great
wrong upon many pure and high-mind
ed women employed In the stores.
Members of the large dry goods Arm of
Wasserman, Kaufman & Co. declared
the chief's statements were too ridic
ulous for .attention.
Coffey stands by his declarations,
however, and says he Is going to call
upon the women's clubs to act on the
alleged evils, and that he intends to
restore the old curfew law and ring a
general alarm bell which will have the
effect of sending all youngsters ( home
and to bed at 8 o'clock.

could give no clew to the actual Iden
tity of those operating the Minnie
Healy on which court summons could

STRIKES GOLD
RIGHT IN TOWN
Old Miner Digging Cellar in
Sparks, Nevada, Uncovers
Quartz of High Value
FIND CAUSES A STIR
Sand Taken From Excava
tion and Panned Yields
Richly in Yellow Dust
Special Dispatch to The Call.
RENO, Nev., Jan. 10.âWhile exca
vating for a cellar in the new railroad
town of Sparks to-day Earnest Vans,
an old miner from California, dug up
several .pieces of gold-bearing quartz
float. This excited him and he con
tinued to dig and in a short time un
covered more rock of the same nature.
The ore is very rich and in some
pieces of it the gold can readily be
seen. The sand in which the rock was
imbedded was black and, upon being
panned, gave high values in the pre
cious metal.
The old man searched for hours for
a ledge, but could find only a narrow
stringer of ore, which gave high values,
but which appeared to be isolated from
any other gold-bearing ore. The dis
covery created considerable excitement
in the town, and also In Reno, but as
the property is owned by A. A. Hib
bard of this city, no claims have been
located as yet. Specimens of the ore
are now on exhibition in this city.
STEAMBOAT STRIKES A ROCK
BUT PASSENGERS ARE SAVED
Dalles City Rushed to Sandbar In
Columbia River and Persons
Aboard Transferred.
PORTLAND. Jan. 10.âThe river
steamboat Dalles City, bound from
thin city to The Dalles, struck a rock
in the Columbia River near Steven
son, Wash., to-day and began to fill.
Ker captain headed the steamboat
toward shore at full speed and man
aged to land on a sandbar. There was
a panic among the seventy "passengers,
but through the efforts of the com
mander and the crew the men and
women pn board were reassured.
The steamboat Regulator took the
passengers off the Dalles City and
proceeded up the river to The Dalles.
A big hole was torn In the Dalles
City's bow and it is feared that she
may go to pieces In the swift waters.

ROOS BROS.
Kearny at Post
VICTORY WON
OVER GAMBLERS
Portland Sheriff Brings the
Ring to Justice and Thus
Ends Long War in Oregon
ACCUSED PLEAD GUILTY
Fines Are Imposed and the
Suits Filed Against the
Officer Are Withdrawn
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PORTLAND, Jan. 10âThe Portland
Club has capitulated and Sherfff Wdrd
has won a victory. Pleas of guilty
have been entered In the gambling
cases, lines imposed, damage cases
against Sheriff Word brought by the
owners of the Portland Club have been
withdrawn and the warfare that has
attracted attention throughout the
entire coast will come to an end. Gam
bling will not henceforth be carried on
at the club, the owners having pledged
themselves to that effect, and the sa
ioon connected therewith will be per
mitted to open.
The most powerful force of the lo
cal army of gamblers has been the
Portland Club, located at Fifth and
Alder streets and owned by Peter
Grant, Nathan Solomon and Harvey
Dale. Two political campaigns have
been fought based largely upon the
arrangement effected with the Port
land Club and the machine, and it has
been believed by informed politicians
that the worsting of the Portland Club
would be the shattering of the ma
chine.
Not alone the Portland Club, but the
other big gambling houses were press
ed to close. Chinese fantan games and
poker games in several places were
raided and arrests made.
Stopped from gambling, the owners
of the Portland Club, In retaliation,
caused the Institution of suits to re
cover large damages, the aggregate be
ing about $50,000. Still later, resistance
on their part continuing, the Sheriff
took away the gambling apparatus
from the Portland Club and stored it
at the Courthouse.

MURDER ENDS
ALASKAN FEUD
Capitalist Robert Ball of.
Seattle Kills William
Deppe, His Former Partner
HIS WEAPON A RIFLE
Slayer Claims to Be Ann
Arbor Graduate and Once
Lived in San Francisco
SEATTLE, Jan. 10.âWord was re
ceived here this evening of the killing
of William Deppe by Robert Ball, on
Fox Island, in Southeastern Alaska,
on the evening of January 2. The
principals in the tragedy were, until
a few days ago, friends and associates
in some marble properties located close
to the scene of the shooting. They
had a business disagreement, which
resulted in a personal encounter in
this city In which Ball was worsted.
Since that time they have been bitter
enemies and it is said each had made
threats to kill the other.
Ball was, until last October, presi
dent of the Great American Marble
Company, a concern capitalized at
$70,000,000. He was deposed in Octo
ber, but he still claimed an Interest in
the property and early last month
sailed for Alaska in order to protect,
as he claimed, his legal rights. Deppe
followed a few weeks later and ar
rived on the Island the night before
the shooting. He went to a cabin ad
joining that of Ball and later went to
the cabin occupied by Ball for the
purpose, as companions who accom
panied him say, of having a talk rela
tive to the claim. Deppe was ordered
out of the place and when he stopped
to remonstrate Ball picked up a rifle
and fired. Deppe fell, dying Aye min
utes later. Deppe's body was taken to
Wrangel, where the authorities were
notified, and officers left for the scene
of the shooting to arrest Ball.
Considerable mystery enshrouds
Ball's past life. He came to this city
about two years ago and launched out
as a capitalist and promoter. He
claimed to be a graduate of the Ann
Arbor College of Law and said that
he had practiced his profession in
New York and San Francisco.
Deppe had been a miner and pros
pector for a number of years and was
well known in Alaska. He came to
this city from lowa.
The hardest thing in the world to
convince a woman of Is that she snores.

WASHINGTON, Jan. 10.âPresident
Roosevelt favors the earliest possible

gress of the tariff. He so Informed the

j were in conference with him at the
i White House last Saturday, and he

j the Fifty-ninth Congress into extra
[â¢ ordinary session as soon as the com
! mittees have indicated that they are
[ prepared to submit a tariff measure
t for passage.
One of those present at the con
! ference on Saturday said to-day in
j view of the many conflicting stories
â¢ published regarding the meeting, that
j the President informed them that,
I while he did not believe in any sweep

! ter that related so particularly to
! them, yet he did think the time had

I thoroughly examined and there should
i be a readjustment.
The President added that his own
I view was that the special session

lat which the committees of the two

llf they took this view, he said, he
! would hold himself ready to call the
â¢ extraordinary session at whatever
! time the members of the two commit
j tees, especially the House Committee
ion Ways and Means, should inform
j him they would be ready with their
! report.
I MAY MEET IN OCTOBER,
If Congress should act on the tariff
' question in accordance with the Presi
i dent's desire, it was made perfectly
plain to-day that the extraordinary
I session would be called to assemble
j the coming spring. It has been point- j
ed out, however, by the tariff experts
of the House and Senate that it will
require many weeks to prepare a ;
; measure for submission to the House, j
: The President realizes that there is a i
' practical objection to an early spring

iof the committees have not the time
new necessary to devote to the prep- j
aration of a tariff bill,
j So far as the President is concerned

lin session during the summer months,
jHe has indicated his entire readiness j
j to remain in Washington next summer
if-Congress will undertake at that time

but Congress, thus far, has shown no

While no decision of a definite char*

onable to believe that if one should be

ably will be in October.
It was added that there is not the

In the party. While the President is
d%eply in earnest In his desire for tariff

clple Is at stake.
TO FIGHT RAILROADS.

ESCAPE AWFUL
DEATH AT SEA
Survivors of the Norwegian
Bark Marpesia Wrecked on
Christmas Day Reach Port
â¢
NEW YORK, Jan 10.âSeven sur
vivor* of the ill-fated Norwegian ship
Marpesia, which was wrecked at sea
Jon Christmas day by an explosion of
! naphtha, resulting in the death of
! eleven members of her crew, arrived
I here to-day on the steamship Trinidad
: from Bermuda. They had been rescued
]by the Danish steamship Gallia, which
happened by just at the 1 moment when
the Marpesia was blown into a shape-.
; less mass. The seven men who sur
vived the shock were taken from the
! sea, wheie they had sought the only
iefuge that remained to them after the
J explosion. They were landed at Ber
muda by the Gallia, which was bound
! from Hamburg for Savannah,
i The Marpesia sailed from New York
i for Cette, France, on December 9 with
' a cargo of naphtha, and had been out
: only a day or two when her troubles
began. One gale succeeded another,
i but all were weathered safely amd
: without much damage until December
| 17. On that day the ship was battered
I and knocked about in a mere iless man-
I ner. Her ventilators were carried
| away at the decks, her hold began to
i fill and the cargo of caEes of naphtha
i began to warp back and forth with
I every movement of the vessel. Soon
! the cases began to give way under the
i strain, and in a little while the freed
j naphtha was floating around on top
of the water in the hold.
Gas from the fluid accumulated
steadily, and on Christmas day the
: Marpesia was a loaded bomb, needing
i only the slightest spark to blow ' the
I structure to atoms. How the spark
j reached the highly charged hold will
never be known, but suddenly there
! came a terrific report, the forward
: decks shot up into the air with a crash
I and a roar, and in a moment the en
tire ship was wrapped in flames. Those
i of the eighteen men on board who had
! not been caught in the first burst
' threw themselves over the side of the
j flaming ship into the sea. There were
â¢ only seven, and the seven would have
i followed their companions to an early
i death had not the Gallia, which
j chanced to be in the vicinity, come to
; their aid.

UNDERWRITERS OF COAST
ASSEMBLE IN CONVENTION
Fuller Suaaests Educational Plan
PRESIDENT OF THE FIRE UNDERWRITERS' ASSOCIATION OF THE PACIFIC.
WHICH OPENED ITS ANNUAL CON VENTION IN THIS CITY YESTERDAT,
AND TWO OF ITS MOST ACTIVE MEMBERS.
At the twenty-ninth annual conven- i
tion of the Fire Underwriters' Asso
ciation of the Pacific, which opened in
this city yesterday. President J. L.
Fuller urged upon the "field men," the
experts connected with the fire under
writing of the Pacific Coast, the tak
ing of a very important step. Some
time ago the executive committee of
the association, which represents a ter
ritory extending from Montana to 1
Texas and the entire Pacific Coast, ad
vocated the formation of a federation
of insurance associations in the United ;
States, similar to those that exist on I
the other side of the Atlantic.
A federation prospers in Great Brit- ;
am and Ireland. The executive com
mittee of the Pacific Association has
already sent invitations to other or
ganizations of insurance men in the
United States, but has received no
respon?e yet to the suggestion that a j
great association shall be formed.
President Fuller reviewed the steps
that have been taken elsewhere. He
said that in comparison with the in
surance institutes of Great Britain and
Ireland none of the insurance organi
zations of the United States have ad
vanced in technical education to the
position reo.uired by the federation.
"Possibly." said President Fuller, "the
time is premature, but technical train
ing in underwriting is needed."
It was time for the subject to be
considered. Incidentally President Ful
ler gave an account of what has been
done elsewhere. This in part is as
follows:
t shall try to point out the way that has
been "blaz<vl" by our brethren tn Oreat
Britain, commencing with the institute of ac
tuaries, which was founded in 1848. While
this iiisUtute, as the nafme implies, is devoted
to life insurance, its existence and workings
influenced the formation of other life insti
tutes and also those combining Are and life,
the first of the latter class being the Institute
of Manchester, which was founded In 1873.
In the course of time nine other combined fire
and life institutes were founded, all em
bodying about the same educational scope,
which includ s a regular course of study and
examination of students.
In, 1897 these institutions, ten In number,
federated under the name of the Federation
of Insurance Institutes of Great Britain and
Ireland, which is the only federation of Insur
ance Institutes' in the world to-day.
The value that is placed upon the privilege
of being leroanlzod by this f-derated body Is
demonstrated by the action of our friends just
across the border line, in Canada, where there
are two thriving institutes, of Montreal and
Toronto. Thes=> two institutions work jointly
In the educat'ohal course and are now prac
tically members of the federated body on the
other side of the water.
PAINTS STRONG PICTURE.
President Fuller, whose address was
filled with practical thoughts from be
ginning to end. strongly recommended
the better education of the younger
insurance men in underwriting and in
whatever appertains to it, and said in
part:
Here we are, one hundred companies strong,
handling a premium income aggregating up
ward of $15,000,000 annually, without the
proper educational safeguard. Just think of
this, too, by way of comparison: The head
office staff of-one company alone constitutes
the active membership of one of the institutes
included in the Federation of Insurance Insti
tutes of Oreat Britain and Ireland. Further
more. Insurance Institute work, so far as I
can learn, is confined to the English speaking
countries of the globe and the United States
Is the only one of them not having this system
of education. Just see where we stand:
Circled around us is Great Britain and Ire
land with eleven institutes, Australia with
four. New Zealand with two. South Africa with
one and Canada with two.
In view of the foregoing, the question now
arises: Must we conUnue In the narrow path,
or shall we broaden our course and try to
keep up with the procession of educated work
ers? If the latter course be decided upon, let
us act now and have it said that the first
recognised insurance Institute founded In the
United States was established under the aus
pices Â©f the Fire Underwriters' Association of
the Pacific.
Long and earnest applause testified
to the favor that was accorded to thef
suggestions made by President Ful*
ler. A committee will consider and j
report to-day and will probably make 1
! suggestions that may lead to some
practical results.
SOME ESSAYS ARE READ.
\ paper W&S read by George M.
Robertson that held, the undivided at
tention of the convention. This was
replete with facts relating to sprink
lers maintained as a protection against
nre in buildings. At the close of the
addiesr questions were asked by the
members of the association, who
were present to the number of about
4u". Mr. Robertson; who was the
principal essayist of the forenoon,
Used vdiagrams to illustrate.
Several entertaining themes were
treated by other members of the asso
ciation in set papers. J. P. Moore
talked about the benefits that a com
plete insurance library hold out to the
studiously inclined underwriters as a
source of necessary information. R.
C. Medcraft spoke about "The Brain
work in Our Business." F. G. Stamf
ord read a paper on "The Careless
H:izard in Our Business"; F. J. Alex
Mayer dealt with "The Adjustment of
Book Losses With and Without
Books," and A. W. Whitmer discussed
"A Sawmill Inspection." To-day
there will be additional papers and
the annual election. This evening the
session will be wound up appropriate
ly with a banquet, at which several
hundred insurance men will be in at
tendance.

Strife Are Confirmed by a
Letter Received in London
LONDON, Jan. 10.âThe active prep
arations already reported to have be
gun in Turkey and Bulgaria for a pos
sible war this year are fully confirmed
by a private letter received in London
from a Minister accredited to the Bal
kan courts. This Minister, who has
Just completed a tour of the Balkan
capitals, writes that the Turkish and
Bulgarian Governments are energetic
ally preparing for eventualities.
Large shipments of arms and ammu
nition have been made, and recruits
from Asia Minor are arriving at Salon
ica weekly for service in the Turkish
army. The Bulgarian and Greek bands
in Macedonia are increasing dally and
are fighting each other and the Turks.
This three-cornered guerrilla warfare
threatens to assume the bloodiest phase
In the spring.
"But," concludes the Minister,
"whether or not there will be real war
fare between Turkey and Bulgaria in
the spring I am not prophet enough to
say. Unless certain powers think the
time la opportune, war may be post
poned this year, as last, but In any
event we are a year nearer the inevit
able conflict."
KAISER'S PRIVATE DENTIST
PUTS AN END TO HIS LIFE
Dr. Sylvester, an American. Sends â¢
Bullet Through His Head
in Berlin..
BERLIN, Jan. 10.âDr. Alonso Syl
vester. Emperor William's American
dentist, committed suicide here to
day. He shot himself through the
head.
The Emperor was fond of Dr. Syl
vester. He created him a royal Prus
sian councilor, appointed him his pri
vate dentist and gave him many pres
ents. Dr. Sylvester was the pioneer
American dentist in Berlin, having
come here thirty years ago. ' He had
an extraordinarily large professional
income, but had nevertheless financial
difficulties. Recently Dr. Sylvester
had been suffering from a severe at
tack of influenza and for two or three
days past he had talked incoherently.
GERMAN COAL MINERS
ON VERGE OF BIG STRIKE
Operators Pursue Course That Is Ex*
pected to Cause Serious Trouble
With Men.
COLOGNE, Germany, Jan. 10.â
Both the coal miners and mine
owners throughout the Rhenish and
Westphalian districts are preparing
for a general strike. So far only 20,
--000 out of a total of 270,000 men con
cerned have gone out. The mine prof
prietors began rejecting requests yes>.
terday. As soon as their replies are
received the miners, according to their
previous decision, do not go to work
when it is time for their next shift.
The employers, under the law fixing
the relation of employer and employe,
are notifying the miners to return to
work or be discharged with the loss
of six days' wages.

in favor of the Oakland Club.MIZPAH POWERFUL
IN MANY LINES.
buffers*a Little
Through Lack
of Age.
In "Mizpah," the Biblical drama by
Ella Wheeler Wilcox and Luscombe
Searelle, produced for the first time
anywhere this week at the Majestic
Theater, the playwrights have at least
chosen a theme of exceptional beauty.
It is the story of Esther, told in four
acts and nine scenes, and in rhymed
and blank verse. Mr. Searelle in a
note to me claims 12S1 of the lines, leav
ing 461 to Mrs. Wilcox's credit. He has
further furnished the incidental music
âthough he objects modestly to being
programmed âas he isâas "the musical
wizard." Mr. Searelle might perhaps
leave that safely to us. Certainly the
musical setting of "Mizpah" cannot be
harged up to any kind of wizardry.
â¢ ' The play itself is an unaffected, inter
sting, somewhat ingenuous and rather
looseiy handled presentation of the
story. It has atmosphere, a certain
< olorful orientalism, but it lacks age.
Its Persia might be the Persia of to
dayâVashti.for example, in her curtain
lecture to Ahasuerus on not being al
lowed to share the king business, is as
much the new woman of the current
comic papers as Carrie Nation. One
.gets again from Vashti a quite modern
dithyramb about the pen's might over
the sword, and so on. And the piece
lacks largely in the ancient sternness
.of fiber. Ahasuerus is more of a
charming philosopher and lover than
'warrior and king. Hainan, too, the bit
terly ambitious, in his presentment
here, with his rather childish inter
ruptions of the king's dicta, becomes
not infrequently something not unlike
the enfant terrible. As a whole, in
short, the picture does not fill the
frame.
The love interest, as might be ex
pected from the "poetess of passion,"
is heavily accented, and happily. Mrs.
Wilcox âsurely these are of the 461â
in these lines and scenes is at her
best. The scene w here Esther comes to
meet the King is the best in the play,
and handled with felicitous delicacy
and sincerity by Miss Adele Block and
J. H. Gilmour. There is the profane
love, too, of Vashti and Haman, clev
erly contrasted, and interpreted to
the' necessary degree of sultriness by
â Eleanor Gordon and Howard Gould.
". Mr. Monahan, of that queer and fla
vorful sheet, the "Papyrus," hails
â¢.Mrs. Wilcox as the leading woman
'writer of the dayâthen proceeds to say
that he means ethicist and philoso
pher. So far as literature goes "Miz
pari" does not establish Mr. Monahan's
claim. The lines have prettiness. occa
sionally strength, even occasionally
poetry, but except here lack wholly in
distinction. However, they run along
easily enough, and tell the story with
commendable clearness; and altogether,
given a scenic setting of adequate
splendorâthe Majestic is excellent for
a stock houseâwith all the luxury and
richness of detail that was expended
upon, say "Ben Hur," quicken the
action of the piece, cut judiciously,
and' you would have a play at least, as
effective as most of the biblical dramas
that have yet made their appearance.
In subject it is more fortunate than
most.
The play owes a good deal to the act
ing. Mr. Gould makes an Ahasuerus
of notable charm, his love scenes with
Esther, as before said, of sincere
and convincing beauty. His reading of
the lines lends much gYace to them.
Miss Block, as Esther, has done noth
ing so good since her Kundry. The
conception has aH the delicate woman
linessâmaidenlinessâand later the full
queenliness essential to the illusion. As
a picture Miss Block is wholly charm
ing. Is not Miss Gordon's Vashti some
what over-shrewish? And would not
her bearing a little more befit a dancer
than a queen? But Miss Gordon suc
ceeds admirably in suggesting the plot
ting and passionate woman, and she is
a stunningly handsome picture. Mr.
Gould is effective in the modern fash
lon as Haman, and Mr. Mac Vicar's
Mordecai has genuine feeling. The
nthers fill in without disaster, the hand
some costumes lending color and splen
dor to the very well-staged piece. Mr.
Searelle's music is occasionally pretty,
hut trivial in the connection.

MYSTERIOUS FIRE GUTS
THREE-STORY BUILDING
â¢ Flames Break Out in Awning Factory
of Ross McMahon and $20,000
Worth of Damage Results.
Fire broke out in the tent and awn
ing factory owned by Ross McMahon,
35 Market street, shortly before 1
o'clock this morning, and before the
blaze was extinguished the three
stories of the building were badly
gutted. The loss to the building and
Its contents will probably reach $20,
--000, including the damage sustained
to the restaurant of Detjen & Men
gel, on the ground floor.
The cause of the blaze is a mys
tery. It was discovered by a watch
man, and two alarms were sent in.
The department did good work and
kept the blaze from spreading to
nearby buildings. No one was in
jured.

FORMER SAN FRANCISCO GIRL
WHO IS WITH THE SILVER
SLIPPER COMPANY.
Dancing Wins
Her Place in
Sextette.
In the ranks of the sextet of dancing
girls, who perform the sensational
champagne dance in the "Silver Slip
per," which will be seen in the Grand
Opera House next week, is a little
American girl who was born and bred
in San Francisco. Her name is Gladys |
Turner, and she will certainly demon
strate that dancers of the highest or- i
der can be found in America. It is no
longer necessary to bring them from
England.
One of the English dancers of the
sextet was taken sick the fore part of
the season and Mr. Fisher was at a loss
to know how to fill her place.
Miss Turner learned of the va
cancy, made application for the
position, was accepted, and it is safe
to say she can hold her own with any
of the other dancers, who are grad
uated from the famous George Ed
wards English school of dancing.
Miss Turner has not been seen iff San
Francisco since she has acquired a
stage reputation. Her return to the
city of her birth will prove a delight to
her many friends.
FAMOUS DIVAS
IN A QUARREL
Madame Eames' Shapely
Hand Smites Face of
Madame Senger Bettaque
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Jan. 10.âAt the re
hearsals of "Lohengrin," in the Metro
politan Opera-house, Mme. Emma
Eames and Mme. Senger Bettaque
quarreled about the superiority and
rank of their respective roles. Mme.
Eames gave her rival a sharp slap on
ihe face.
"Ladies!" called out the director.
Mme. Senger Bettaque began
searching in her pocket, but when
her hand came into view happily it
held nothing more formidable than a
book of rules compiled by Frau
Cosima Wagner for the festival house
at Bayreuth. One rule provided:
"If the soprano should slap the face
of the contralto the soprano should
apologize on her knees to the contral
to and pay fine of 500 marks."
"You shall apologize on your knees
to me, M'r=s Touch-Me-Xot," she con
tinued, "and pay $100, Miss Yankee."
"You are not a contralto. I would
apologize if you were. And we are
not at Bayreuth." said Mme. Eames.
"And I shall not go on my knees ex
cept to thank the Lord that I am a
Yankee instead of a Dutch woman."
But Mme. Eames was finally made
to apologize, nevertheless.
BANKERS SAY THAT STORY
OF SALE IS UNTRUTHFUL
Bank of California Has Not Bought
London and San Francisco
Bank, Limited.
Manager Anderson of the Bank of
California and William Mackintosh,
manager of the London and San Fran
cisco Bank, Limited, denied positively
yesterday that there was any truth
in a published story that the Bank of
California had purchased the London
and San Francisco Bank. Manager
Mackintosh said that the statement re
garding the sale that has appeared In
print was mendacious and asserted
that there has been no sale to either
the Bank of California or any other !
bank. He also said that the story of
sale was ridiculous for the reason that
directors of British banks cannot sell
without the consent of the share
holders. More than one-third of the
shares of the London and San Fran
cisco Bank are held in this city. Hence
there could be no sale, as it would be
necessary to give notice publicly to
the shareholders before asking their
consent to such a transaction.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL', WEDNESDAY. JANUARY 11, 1905.
LONDON SHOWN
ON THE CANVAS
Burton Holmes' Travelogue
on Great Metropolis Pleases
Vast Audience at Lyric Hall
i
â¢TALK IS INSTRUCTIVE
â¢
i Pictures of Queen Victoria
and King Edward Are
Cheered With Enthusiasm
I â¢
'"In London" was the travelogue of
' Burton Holmes at Lyric Hall last
' night. The familiar scenes of the
world's great metropolis were loudly
j applauded as they rolled upon the
! canvas.
For those who have had the good for
tune to visit that wonderful city the
exquisite views at last night brought
I back the scenes with great vividness.
;It w r as almost like being in London
again. For those who had never been
in that city the travelogue was a liber
al and thorough education.
The 154 scenes displayed represented
selections from every portion of that
greac city. West End, East End, slums,
houses of parliament, parks, museums,
palaces, law courts, Bank of England,
precessions and galleries were all ex
quisitely illustrated by pictures that
were the very gems and perfection of
the photographers' art.
Many of these were exquisitely col
ored. Besides these, there were nine
teen motion pictures. They represent
led coronation processions, Petticoat
! Lane, the East End Ghetto, Hyde Park,
May fair, the Sunday church parade,
j changing of the guard at St. James
i Palace and the trooping of the color.
The picture of the precession of
i Queen Victoria's jubilee evoked much
j enthusiasm. As het Majesty appeared
;in an open carriage bowing to the
j throngs of people that lined the streets
; the audience broke into prolonged
; cheering.
The picture representing King Ed
; ward on his way to the coronation was
a splendidly arranged affair. As the
King appeared in the royal gilded
| chariot, drawn by twelve magnificent
white Arab chargers, he received a
great volley of applause. Lord Roberts
was also similarly honored, as he ap
peared In one of the passing pageants.
The lecture or talk of Mr. Holmes
I was in itself a great treat. He graph
ically described every picture in a high
|ly comprehensive manner. Dashes of
: sparkling humor were frequently in
' terspersed throughout his interesting
, remarks. The hall was packed to its
greatest capacity. Applause was fre
quent and spontaneous. "In London"
: will be repeated again to-night, and
next to seeing London itself, comes
Burton Holmes with his travelogue.
CHRISTIAN ASSOCIATION
ENTERTAINS ITS FRIENDS
Basket Ball Games and Wrestling
Contests Excite the Large Crowd
In Attendance.
The Young Men's Christian Associa
tion gymnasium held an overflowing
crowd last night on the occasion of
the first ladies' evening given by that
organization this year. The enter
tainment provided for the guests con
sisted mainly of athletics, the features
of which were several wrestling
matches and basket ball contests be
tween teams of young women ' and
young men. In the wrestling tourna
ment R. E. Barney of the Y. M. C. A.
and C. D. Pentony of the Olympic
Club strove for the lightweight hon
ors, the former being awarded the de
cision. In the middleweight contest
G. F. Anloff of the Reliance Club of
Oakland defeated Edward Mistelskl
of the Y. M. C. A. In the heavy- j
weight match Emil Tshumi of the
Olympic Club scored over George Pi- 1
per of the Y. M. C. A.
An event of the evening that
aroused much interest was the contest
between the young ladies' basket ball
teams of San Mateo and San Fran
cisco, ending in a hard earned victory
for the San Mateo maidens, with a
score of 14 to 6. In the basket ball
game between the Titans and the Re
liance Club team the first half ended
in a score of 9 to 7 for the latter, but
the final score was changed to 18 to 10
in favor of the Titans. Between the
main events of the evening the ladies'
class of the association gave an in
teresting drill and a trio, composed
of William Hewer, Benjamin Mor
gan and Harry Weingard, entertained
the gathering with a number of acro
batic feats.
WILL ASSIGN SUBSTITUTES
TO REGULAR POSITIONS
Board of Education finds Tliat Seven
New Teachers Will Be Required
For Xew School Term.
The Board of Education was busy
yesterday arranging the transfers and
assignments of teachers for the open
ing of the new term of school next
Monday. After assigning some
twenty teachers who were on leaves
of absence the board found that It
would have to take seven teachers
from the substitute list and elect them
to regular positions In the depart
ment. A meeting was to have been
held to carry this out, but it was
agreed to postpone formal action until
the regular meeting to-morrow after
neon. The seven substitutes first on
the list are Geraldine Boyle, Mabel
Stuart. Grace Sullivan, Mabel Miller,
May Kerns and Lillian Clarkson.
A new class in the Chinese school
will be formed.
Thomas F. Egan, who was appoint
ed Commissioner of Public Works,
filed his resignation as storekeeper of
the Board of Education. As the board
did not meet no action was taken on
the resignation.

SLAVE GIRLS SENT
BACK TO CHINA
Moon Tin Escapes a Policeman Armed
With a Warrant For Her Arrest
For Larceny.
Ah Wong and Ah Lee. Chinese
women, were deported on the steam
ship China yesterday. They had at
tempted to gain admission to this
country on the representation that
they were the wives of merchants
domiciled here. They were denied a
landing and took an appeal to the
Secretary of the Department of Com
merce and Labor. Pending the ap
peal they confessed that they were
slaves. On the same vessel went Woon
Tin. a slave girl who had been res
cued from her keepers by Mies
Wheeler of the Presbyterian Mission.
Just before the steamer started a i
policeman arrived with a warrant for 1
her arrest on a charge of larceny. He
brought with him a Chinaman who
was to point out the girl, but the
Customs Inspectors would not allow .
the Chinaman to go on board, so the
girl escaped.

COILS TIGHTEN
AROUND WYMAN
Testimony of Expert Kytka
Is Strong Blow to Case
of the Alleged Stuffer
SIGNATURE IS DENIED
! S. 11. Mann Declares That He
| Did Not Vote at Elec
tion Held on August 9
The strongest testimony yet submit
ted against Charles Wyman, accused
of voting fraudulently, was presented
yesterday in Judge Lawlor's court by i
Theodore Kytka, handwriting expert.
Kytka declared in a positive tone that j
the name of S. H. Mann on the roster |
of the Seventy-third Precinct of the
Thirty-ninth District was in the hand
writing of the defendant, Charles Wy- !
man.
When the prosecuting attorney asked
the question, "Did the same person who
wrote this affidavit write S. H. Mann, j
26078 Post street," Wyman craned
forward in his seat and a hush fell j
over the courtroom. "Yes, sir," replied !
Kytka, decidedly. Wyman drooped his '
head and paled slightly. The strongest
bolt of the prosecution had reached
home.
The expert started to show the points
from which he drew his concusllons by
means of a blackboard, but darkness
Intervened. He drew a fac simile of j
the W in the ordinary Wyman slgna- j
ture on the blackboard and compared
It with the M in Mann.
"The initial strokes in both letters I
are identical," declared Kytka. "The M '
1n Mann is the characteristic Wyman j
W with a final stroke."
"See," he exclaimed, dramatically, "I
erase the final stroke and we have the
Wyman W." The roster containing the
alleged signature of S. H. Mann and
examples of Wyman's handwriting
were passed to the jurors.
S. H. Mann took the stand early in
the afternoon and declared that he did
.not enter his name in the roster of the
Seventy-third Precinct on August 9. In
the cross-examination Mann admitted
(hat he had been employed by Fairfax
Wheelan subsequent to the primary l
election. Attorney Countryman offered
as evidence a receipt for $3 obtained
from Wheelan and bearing Mann's sig
nature.
"Why did you hire this man?" was
asked Fairfax Wheelan.
"I wanted to make sure that he
would not be captured by the defense,"
replied Wheelan.. "Besides, I wanted .
to get an v authentic example of his
handwriting."
"At the time you hired him did you
speak to him about the election?"
"I did not," replied Wheelan, posi
tively.
"Did you ever see him before August
9?" inquired Countryman.
"I did not." was the" answer. ,
Fair fax Wheelan's son was on the
stand in the morning and substantiated
the testimony his father gave on the
previous day. Special Policeman Jo
seph Doyle said that he saw Wyman
vote on the afternoon of August 9, but
did not know whether he voted in his
own name or used the name of S. H.
Mann.
A. R. Goechen, the ballot clerk of
the Seventy-third Precinct, declared
that ballot No. 31,328 had been voted
but said that he did not know who ]
voted it. During the examination of j
this witness Attorney Hosmer became j
impatient at Countryman's frequent ob- i
jections and displayed some temper. 1
The case will be resumed at 9:30 a. m. j
to-day, when Kytka will illustrate on j
the blackboard why he believes Wyman |
wrote the name of S. H. Mann.

Patrolman Dwyer's Mistake.
Patrolman "Jerry" Dwyer had the
dissatisfaction of seeing three men
whom he arrested dismissed by Judge
Mogan. Acting on the complaint of S.
Polemus of 137 Larkin street, who al
leged that he had been assaulted by
Harry Quig and Harry La Croux, the
officer went to the Fulton House, man
aged by ex-Superintendent of Streets
W. W. Ackerson, and there arrested
Quig and La Croux, and also took in
Ackerson for interfering. The Judge
held that the policeman had no right
to arrest Quig and La Croux without
having a warrant, as he did not wit
ness the battery complained of by
Polemus: also, that Ackerson was jus
tified in attempting to prevent the ille
gal arrests In his house. Officer Dwyer
has been long enough on the force to
know better than to do what he did.

PROPOSITION
TO BUILD BIG
CLUBHOUSE
The newest thing in feminine club
! dom is the erection of a large clubhouse
' that will subserve the purposes of all
the interested clubs in town, of which
: thera is a goodly number.
For some time Miss Ednah Robinson
j and other clever club women have been
j looking into the need and the advisa-
I bility of a general home, with a large
auditorium, cozy clubrooms, with ac
cessories for comfort and a gymnasium
and a swimming tank within.
Yesterday the presidents of nearly all
the clubs in town met and conferred
with a number of business men about
the feasibility of the plan.
The idea was unanimously approved.
| The location of the proposed building,
; however, was a matter of strong differ
ence of opinionâwhether it should be
east or west of Powell street.
The matter was laid over till the,
next conference.
The most interesting phase of the
project is the proposition to have a
swimming tank and a gymnasium ex
clusively for women.
That the matter will crystallize rÂ»to
something tangible is assured.

I 1 122-132 SUTTER ST.
Child Restored to Father.
By order of Judge Lawlor Ellen
Harvey, the 11-year-old girl who was
kidnaped from r.edding by her
mother, was restored to the father,
William Harvey, yesterday. The Har
veys were divorced in 1897, and the
father was given the custody of four
children. Mrs. Harvey is now Mrs.
A. M. Newberg of this city. She al
leges that the father treats the chil
dren brutally and gave that as an ex
cuse for kidnaping the little girl. The
mother acquiesced in the decision of
the Judge, but declares she will take
the proper steps to obtain the custody
of little Ellen. William S. Barnes ap
peared for the father and Police Com
missioner Hutton for the mother.
