NEWS OF THE COUNTY OF ALAMEDA
CREMATORY EXPLOSION
STARTLES WEST OAKLAND
TRY TO BLOW UP GARBAGE PLANT
/ Workmen's Lives Endangered by Fiend
. OAKLAND, Jan. 10.âLives of half
a dozen men were endangered and a
portion of the works was destroyed
last .night In a dastardly attempt to
blow up the plant of the Pacific In
â¢ icinerating Company at West Oakland.
â¢ A keg of powder was surreptitious
ly placed in one of the large combustion
â¢furnaces used to burn refuse. The
â¢ stuff exploded after the fires had been
started, partly wrecking the big brick
furnace, blowing heavy iron caps off
the air vents, smashing the large Iron
grate bars and sending a rain of brick
and iron over the men who were work
ing on the platform above the fur
.' naces.
For weeks the employes of the com
pany had been closely, watching for
some such culmination of the bitter
warfare that has been waged between
the company and the scavengers. The
officials of the plant admit that they
have no proof as to the perpetrators of
the powder plot, although their sus
picions are directed against individ
. uals because of threats which have
been repeated as having come from
disgruntled persons.
CALL FOR BIDS
ON NEW SCHOOLS
Board of Education Moves
Toward the Construction
of First of the Edifices
â¢ Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 10.
The Board of Education has called
for bids for construction of the foun
dation of the new Franklin school in
East Oakland, the first of the buildings
'. to be erected with money raised by the
. recent $1,000,000 school bond issue.
Work will be pressed as rapidly as is
consistent with the construction of a
first-clas6 edifice. The new school will
cost 1150,000.
Superintendent of Schools J. W. Mc-
Clymonds made a statement to-day
. touching the board's purpose in the
matter of new school buildings. He
said:
Work on the Franklin School, the new
building on Shattuck avenue, near Bushrod
Park, and the new edifice at Thirty-ninth and
Market streets, will be commenced at once.
The Board of Education will push the arrange
ments without delay. The new Lincoln School
cannot be started until summer vacation, be
cause It wilt occupy the site of the present
structure. Before work on the new one can
be commenced the old building will be moved.
Within a short tim* work will begin on the
additions to the Lafayette and the Grant
schools. By the opening of the summer vaca
tion we shall be roady not only to go ahead
with the Lincoln School, but with the Prps
cott and the Grove-street building. So far
.as conditions appear at present, all of this
new work should he well under way this year.
Mrs. Frank Day has resigned as a
teacher at the Central Evening School.

THAT POLKS BE REMOVED
*. Crnsade Against Unsightly Signs
Causes the Passage of Resolu
tion Regarding Wires.
OAKLAND, Jan. 10.âMembers of
â¢the Merchants' Exchange at a meet
ing last night expressed themselves
1' ' regarding the enforcement of the or
â¢ dinance ridding the streets of signs in
the following resolution, which was
unanimously adopted:
Whereas, The merchants of Oakland, at the
request of the Mayor, have removed from the
s'dewalks of this city sisns costing thousands
ot dollars; and
Whereas. The same streets and sidewalks
are occupied by poles and wires far more
unsightly and dangerous than any signs could
possibly be; therefore be it
Resolved, by the Merchants' Exchange of
Oakland, That the city authorities are hereby
requested to take immediate action to cause
the removal from the streets and sidewalks
of all such obstructions as soon as It may be
pi*sible to secure such removal.
Resolved. That a special committee of five
be appointed to confer with the Board of
Public Works concerning this important mat
ter.
In accordance with this resolution
President Jonas appointed a commit
tee consisting of Directors D. C.

NOT eCNUINC WITHOUT MY eiSMATURC:
ci o prince ST., New York.
Write ron Fru Booklet on Batiowai. Triatmsnt
or DISIABC
The explosive was Introduced by be
ing thrown into a wagon load of refuse
while the team was on its rounds.
When the load was emptied into the
furnace the can of powder went
with it.
Shortly after the mass had been fired
the explosion occurred. The shock was
felt for half a mile around the works.
No damage was done outside of the
plant because of its isolated location.
It is the practice in handling the re
fuse to use one of the big furnaces ex
clusively for garbage. The other one
is reserved for the incineration of
other combustible matter. The powder
could not have been so readily placed
m the garbage furnace without detec
tion.
Frank Melbourne, superintendent of
the plant, declares that he has good
reason to believe that the explosive
was intended to wreck the plant. That
it did not fully accomplish the mis
creant's purpose was due to the size
of the furnace and the fact that the
force of the explosion was somewhat
broken by escape of gases through
vents.
HARRISON WINS
BY TWO VOTES
Republicans Elect Three of
the Five Supervisors in
Contra Costa County
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 10.
For the first time in twelve years the
Board of Supervisors of Contra Costa
County is controlled by a Republican
majority.
The election of Ralph Harrison, Re
publican, from the Second District, was
finally assured to-day by the recount
of the ballots held before Judge Wells,
to settle the contest brought by F. W.
Booth, Harrison's opponent. The offi
cial returns showed that he had been
elected by one vote, while by the re
count he gained a vote. The result of
the recount shows that Harrison re
ceived 2tÂ»o votes. Booth 288.
The new Board of Supervisors to-day
made the following appointments:
County Phyisician.A. W. Rickey, M. D.;
superintendent of County Hospital, W.
A. Rugg; night watchman at court
house, VV. A. Livingston; janitors in
courthouse, James Daley and William
Jones; county expert, A. P. Pavollni,
allowance clerk, Miss May Kelly.
The position of inspector of weights
and measures, filled by former Assem
blyman Arthur Williams, was declared
vacant, and no appointment was made
Ito fill the vacancy.

ESCAPES SENTENCE.âOakIand. Jan. 10.â
Habeas corpus proceedings freed Henry Rob
erts from serving a sentence of 150 days in
the City Prison this morning. His attorneys,

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY. JANUARY 11, 1905.
Steps have been taken to run down
some clews that the company has un
covered, although the officials refuse
to say what they have learned con
cerning the identity of suspected per
eons. Melbourne said to-day:
We have long been aware ot the bitter feel
ing that exists among the scavengers concern
ing the crematory. The company which, un
der municipal ordinance, holds the contract
for exclusive handling of the city's garbage,
has tried unavallingly to make peaceful set
tlements with these men. We offered to buy
their outfits and to put them at work. After
all negotiations failed ws decided to assert
our rights in the oourts.
As a result there have been many arrests
of scavengers. The courts have been crowded
with cases. So little progress was made that
the company went to the United States oourts
to obtain injunctions against further operations
by the scavengers. This move undoubtedly
added to the feeling. I cannot fix the re
sponsibility for this outrage. I have been
afraid for months that dynamite might be
used and have been especially careful. I
hardly expected powder would be used in such
quantity as was placed in the furnace.
It's bad enough to wreck property, but I
say that terms are not strong enough to de
nounce deeds that endanger men's lives. I
serve notice now that if the man or men are
caught in the act it will be shortshift for
them so far as I am concerned.
SAYS FARMER
IS ADVANCING
President Wheeler Talks
About the Progress of Ag
riculture as a Science
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street, Jan. 10.
Profoundly impressed by the develop
ment of agriculture on scientific lines
in California and by the keen, intelli
gent interest manifested in the work of
that department of the State Univer
sity, Benjamin Ide Wheeler, president
of the University of California, returned
to-day from a ten days' visit among the
farmers of Santa Barbara and Ventura
counties.
President Wheeler delivered an ad
dress at the Farmers' Institute at San
ta Paula, where he was in touch with
many of the leading agriculturists of
that section. His comment on his trip
follows:
The farmers of California are becoming pro
fessional men. The wide interest they are
manifesting In the scientific side of agricul
ture is becoming an important factor in the
work of tilling the soil and growing the diver
sified crops of this empire State. The farmers'
I appreciation of the importance of expert
knowledge is one of the most noticeable re
suits of the university's farmers' institutes.
This is demonstrated by the clean-cut observa
tions that are made at the lectures and the
enthusiastic concern of the audiences at the
institutes.
I remarked the careful and intelligent
thought shown by the queries of listeners at
Santa Paula. Professor Fortier was fairly
bombarded with questioTn* after he had con
cluded an address that was on cold scientific
lines. The farmer of California and of the
United States has at his command through this
great university a source of knowledge un
equaled the world over. In Europe and In
Great Britain there Is no such attention' paid
to agricultural development as is glv> n to
farming In this country. I am glad to ob
serve that our farmers realize ths needs of
the university and understand the importance
of its relation to our agricultural interests.
During his absence President Wheel
er had a conference with Governor
Pardee touching the financial interests
of the university. Of that President
Wheeler said:
Governor Pardee is In touch with our needs
and when the appropriations are made I feel
satisfied we shall receive all that can possibly
be allotted to us. Of course we appreciate
that the coat must be cut according to the
cloth.
MARIE ACKERMAN FOUND
TO BE VIOLENTLY INSANE
A Week's Residence Among Her
Friends Shows Them She Is
Hopelessly Crazy.
OAKLAND, Jan. 10.âA week's resi
dence among them was enough to
demonstrate to the friends of Miss
Marie Ackerman that she is really
j insane. The Rev. F. W. Meyer of
j East Oakland took an interest in her
| case and it was thought that owing
j to her being unable to speak the lan
guage, having recently come from
; Switzerland, she had been misjudged.
I Judge Melvin, to give her another op-
I portunity, set her commitment to
! Stockton aside and allowed her to go
j with her friends.
i This morning the Rev. Mr. Meyer
1 appeared before Judge Melvin and
asked to have her again taken into
custody. He said she had been with
his family a week and that they
could do nothing with her. She be
lieves a man is a.'ter her, and when
Deputy Sheriff Murdock went to take
her into custody she fought like a
tigress. She is again confined in the
insane ward and will be committed toy
morrow.

; home, 2207 Adeline street, aged 60

j lowa, and was a veteran of the Civil

LIQUOR SELLER
DOWN AND OUT
Temperance Folk of Univer
sity Score Victory and
Sam Fisher Is Defeated
WAR CLOUD DISSIPATED
Downfall of West Berkeley
Man Averts Threatened
Fight to Close Saloons
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street, Jan. 10.
The threatened agitation by temper
ance advocates of Berkeley of a cam
paign to close all the saloons In the
university town is not to be. since
"Sam" Fisher, the casus belli in West
Berkeley, has been denied a liquor li
cense by the Town Trustees.
Fisher, with his supporters, appeared
at the meeting of the Trustees last
night, and arrayed against him were
the W. C. T. U. folk and other tem
perance people. It had been declared
by them that if Fisher, now a liquor
seller in West Berkeley, should receive
a formal permit to dispense intoxicants,
at once the agitation for the closing
down of all saloons in Berkeley would
begin.
Fisher achieved notoriety a year ago,
when he attacked a "rummage sale"
which ladies of the Methodist church
were conducting in the storeroom ad
joining his saloon on University av
enue. He broke up the sale. He was
arrested, charged with battery, tried
and acquitted. He lost his liquor li
cense, but co'ntlnued to do business. He
applied for a formal permit, and last
night received his quietus.
Two Trustees, composing the commit
tee to which his application had been
referred, reported favorably on the
petition. The Trustees were Dowd and
Hoff. Trustee Staats was expected to
join them, but instead he advocated
appropriating $500 to pay detectives
who should procure evidence to convict
"such men as Fisher" who were de
clared to be selling liquor without li
censes.
SOCIETY FOLK
HOLD A CLOWN
FETE AT HOME
BY ZOE GREEN RADCLIFFE.
OAKLAND, Jan. 10.âThe members, and
their respective husbands, of a five hundred
club were entertained last evening by Mr. and
Mrs. J. "Walter Scott. Each guest had been
requested to represent some ridiculous charac
ter and tho motley gathering that resulted
was the source of several hours of unalloyed
fun.
The hostess. In quaint, old fashioned garb,
started the" merriment aa she received each
guest. Mrs. W, A. So brock, with -#lrty face
and sticky hands, was an e'rtfant terrible, and
Mrs. Fred Morse, in black face and kinky,
braided wig. simply convulsed her fellcw guests
as Topsy. Another very funny costume was
worn by Mrs. H. B. Mehrmann, who repre
sented the regulation Irish woman of the stage,
in a bright red â¢ jersey," and ill-hanging skirt
and gorgeous hosiery.
Mrs. M. J. Laymance was "Sally in Our
Alley," and Mrs. Ernest Boyes and Mrs. Gil
bert Curtiss were both old-fashioned girls.
Mrs. Ernest Boyes, in an Immense hoopsklrt,
succeeded In being "always In the way," and
Mrs. â¢R. C. Craft, as a tough girl, certainly
made a hit. Mrs E. B. Davis was in fancy
dress.
Kot all of the gentlemen were in costume,
but those who were furnished fun enough for
all who were not. Mr. Scctt was a hunter,
Mr. Laymance a cowboy, Mr. Schrook a
farmer. Dr. Morse as Weary Willie, E. E.
Davis as a tramp and Dr. Boyes as a Dutch
comedian were a whole show in themselves. Mr.
Craft in a fisherman's costume and Mr. Curtlss
as a very ridiculous dude were also very funny.
A game of five hundred was played and the
prizes, as comical as the costumes worn, were
won by Mrs. Ernest Boyes, Mr. Craft and Mrs.
Laymance.
A bucket of water, served with a bright,
new tin dipper, furnished liquid refreshment
during the early evening, but later, when tho
tramp party was ushered to the basement,
substantial alleviation of both hunger and
thirst was found. There were tables covered
with brown paper, wooden plates heaped with
Dutch delicacies, beer In thick glasses, and
delicious coffee In still thicker cups. There
were boxes for the guests to sit on. The
name cards were very original and made pretty
souvenirs of the occasion. Tag cards, such as
are used by the express companies, were
adorned with a hobo picture and the name of
a guest written thereon.
This particular club Is one of the Jolliest In
town, and every time It meets the hostess has
some surprise In store for her associates.

FOOD SECRETS
NOW REVEALED
University Professor Prints
Result of His Investiga
tions Into Fruit Values
DISPELS COMMON ERROR
Scientist Declares People
Who Eat Fruit Just for
Fun Make Gross Blunder
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street, Jan. 10.
University students who live on
fruits, nuts and other articles of diet
prescribed by Professor M. E. Jaffa,
with other adults carrying out similar
instructions, furnished the medium for
scientific investigations of food values,
the results of which are contained in
a compilation of bulletins, issued to-day
by the agricultural department of the
State University.
Under the head of "Nutrition Inves
tigations" the university scientist gives
elaborate tables, showing In detail, by
figures, what proportion of nutriment
and life-giving power the various arti
cles of diet were found to possess. The
experiments were made on the univer
sity campus in buildings specially ar
ranged for the work. All the work
was done in co-operation with the
United States Department of Agricul
ture.
Because of California's fame as a
fruit-producing State it was deemed
desirable by those in charge of the
matter to make the examination of
foods deal principally with fruits.
Concerning this phase of the subject,
Professor Jaffa says:
Fruit is considered by the majority of per
sons as an accessory or supplementary food,
eaten for its agreeable flavor or supposed
hygienic value or medical virtues, rather
than as a staple article of diet. Perhaps for
this reason very little scientific study has
b<-en given to fruit, as compared with the
investigations which have been carried on in
count ction with other more common food ma
tennis. Chemical analysis has shown the
comparative composition of fruits, but our
knowledge of their dietetic value, digestibility
and comparative cost as sources of nutriment
is far from complete. In view of these facts
it was thought best that California should
undenake. as her phare of the nutrition in
vestigations made under the auspices of the
United State Department of Agriculture,
studies of the nutritive value and digestibility
of fruit. Perhaps no State in the/ Union
Ik in better condition to exploit such prob
lems. No month In the year finds the Cali
fornia market without fresh fruit of local
production and many people are to be found
in the State who make, this article an im
portant rart of their dietary.
VARIOUS BELIEFS.
It Is not an infrequent occurrence to see
popular articles in the dally papers and mag
azines which assert the superiority of the
vegetarian diet over the ordinary diet, or the
fruitarian over the vegetarian. The fruitar
ians claim, with some degree of justice, that
the so-called vegetarians are not true vege
tarians, but arc simply non-meat eaters. This
is certainly true of many of them, as a large
proportion Include animal products In their
dietaries, since they consume considerable
quantities of milk, butter, cheese, eggs, etc.,
and some even eat fish.
In inaugurating our Investigations It was
deemed desirable to select such subjects as
were not only accustomed to the larse use
of fruit, but whose dietaries did not contain
many other foods, fortunately subjects were
found Â» ,l to lived almost exclusively on raw
fruits and nuts.
The Investigations of fruitarians Included
(1) the study t f six dietaries whi -h, with two
exceptions were made up of fruits and nuts
exclusively (In the first case the subject ate
a small quantity of a cereal preparation, ana
in the second some green vegetables); t2) one
digestion experiment with a girl. In which
fruits and nuts constituted the entire diet;
(3) a study of the income and outgo ot nitro
gen in the digestion experiment.
A review of the results of the studies
of the fruit and nut diet is as follows,
the summary being the work of Pro
fessor Jaffa:
The high nutritive value of the fruitarian
diet Is shown most clearly in the case of th?,
university student who, though enUrely un
accustomed to such tare, gradually changed
from an ordinary mixed diet to one of fruit
and nuts without loss of health or strength.
He was able for eight days to carry on his
ksim] college work and also for part of the
time to Derform heavy physical work on an
exclusively fruitarian diet, without any ma
terial loss in weight.
While some of the dietaries prove con
clusively that it is possible to obtain the
requisite amount of protein and a proper fuel
value from a fruitarian diet, still the major
ity of those studied fell far below the tenta
tive standards. At the same time It Is not
just to ascribe this failure to the form of
diet. These same people might have con
sumed no larger quantities of the nutrients
on a mixed diet.
It would seem from the data that It was
more difficult for two of the subjects to ob
tain the requisite amount of protein when
on a limited diet of one kind of nut com
bined with fruits than it was when they
were unrestricted and ate of a variety of
each.
WANTED OTHER FOODS.
In nearly all cases where the diet was
limited in variety, the sub ect complained of
a constant craving for something else, a
green vegetable or a cereal. At such times
it was found that th* coefficients of availabil
ity were lower than those recorded when the
subject ate some little "relish" with the
fruits and nuts.
The dietaries which Included cereals yielded
better results In most cases than those which
were limited strictly to fruits and nuts.
The chief sources of nutriment in fruit are
the carbohydrates, which from the results of
the metabolism experiments are shown to
possess a coefficient of availability only slight
ly lower than that of the carbohydrates of the
mixed diet. ThiÃ would emphasize the fact
that fruit should be regarded as a valuable
part of such a diet.
The nltrsgen equilibrium in the body was
maintained in fourteen out of the thirty-one
direstion experiments, and in some cases thit
to done on a smaller amount of protein
than as usually consumed by the same sub
ject during the dietary studies.
The cost per person per day of the nine
dietary studies varied from 18 tents to 4(1
cents. '
While the writer deems It advisable to wait
until more data have been gathered before
publishing coefficients of availability for tho
different fruits and nuts, enough work has
been done to prove that they have a much
hiuher nutritive value than Is popularly at
tributed to them. And it Is certainly an error
to use nuts merely as an accessory to an al
ready heavy meal and to regard fruit merely
from the supposed hygienic or medicinal stand
point.

TO REORGANIZE
POLICE FORCE
Change of Men and Method
Contemplated by the Offi
cials of Town of Berkeley
MARSHAL MAKES REPORT
All Night Service in Office
Ordered by Trustees to
Afford Better Protection
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street. Jan. 10.
A complete reorganization of the po
lice force of Berkeley is expected to
follow the recommendations made by
Marshal Kerns to the Town Trustees
last night, in the event of a. favorable
report from the committee to which
they were referred for consideration.
The permaneut addition of five men
to the force already employed, the in
stallation of an all-night service in
the Marshal's office, the uniforming of
the staff and a system of reports such
as prevails in metropolitan police de
partments are some of the results cal
culated upon by those who are work
ing to strengthen the Berkeley con
stabulary.
Marshal Kerns recommended last
night that he be given a new deputy,
who should serve exclusively In his of
fice at night. It was explained that
citizens now have difficulty in finding a
policeman at night when one is need
ed, as Marshal Kerns is not able to re
main at one place all night, and his
deputies are patrolling the streets. The
request was granted, and hereafter all
deputies will report each half-hour to
the office.
The Marshal desires that the four
men recently given him be retained as
permanent members of his force. These
men would doubtless be uniformed.
The Trustees will have presented to
them arguments for the retention of
the men, and it is believed that a fa
vorable report from the committee will
follow.

PARROT IN CUSTODY.âOakland, Jan. 10.
The trade of a parrot that talks for two
bottles of whisky and some cigars of question
able quality has caused the arrest of bird and
alleged owner. Both are confined In the
County Jail for lack of ball. The arrest of
the bird and G. J. Renshaw was made at the
request of P. J. Deluca, proprietor of the Idle
Hour Saloon, near Emeryville, who alleges he
traded the whisky to Renshaw's son for the
bird. Renshaw says that the lad Is but 10
years of age and had no right to the bird.

NEW SORORITY INSTALLED
AT STANFORD UNIVERSITY
Chapter of Gamma Phi Beta Formally
Established, With Membership
of Fourteen.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY. Jan. 10.
The national college Greek letter aor
ority Gamma Phi Beta was Installed
at Stanford last evening, absorbing
a local chapter known aa Gamma
Beta. The installation was in charge
of the Eta Chapter from Berkeley,
but there were also representatives
present from the Beta. Chapter of
Michigan, the Gamma Chapter of
Wisconsin and the Theta Chapter of
Denver. The Stanford girls who were
initiated into the sorority are:
Jeraldine Brown. '04, San Joee: Husl Pat
terson, '05. Los Angeles: Helen Dorrance, '05,
Carpenterla; Helen Salisbury, '06, Ix>s Ange
les: Mllola Ward. '06, Portland, Ore.; Ruth
Gilbert '06, Palo Alto: Emma Charlebols.
'00, Ventura; Helen Thoburn, 07, Palo Alto;
Pauline Gartimann. '07. Long Beach; Jessica
Bird. '08, Banning; Mabel Gray, '08. Albany.
N. V.; Mabel Crow, '08. Al ham bra; Georgia
Mullen, '08. Cedar Rapids, la.; Winifred Gil
bert, '08. Palo Alto.
The local chapter of Gamma Beta
was formed two years ago by a num
ber of girls in Roble Hall who felt
that there was room for another
sorority at Stanford. Gamma Phi
Beta was petitioned to for a charter
at once and one was granted on De
cember 28, 1904. The local chapter
will be known as the Mv Chapter of
Gamma Phi Beta.
WASHOUTS IN ARIZONA
DELAY ALL TRAFFIC
Neither the Southern PaclflcJ'noT the
Santa Fe Able to Run on
Schedule.
PHOENIX, Ariz., Jan. 10.âThe big
rainstorm continues, the total pre
cipitation for the two days being near
ly three Inches, or more than half as
much rain as fell here during the en s
tire year of 1904. Northbound Santa
Fe, Prescott and Phoenix trains were
delayed for a few hours to-day by a
washout near Wlckenburg. -The
bridge over the Gila River at Kelvin,
on the Phoenix and Eastern, was dam
aged considerably, and eastbound
Southern Pacific trains a.*e delayed
by washouts between Yuma and Mari
copa.
All the rivers are booming and
canals are full. Cave Creek over
flow Inundated the town of Glendale,
nine miles east of Phoenix, doing
some damage. The benefits to the
country will vastly exceed any possible
damage.
SAN DIEGO, Jan. 10.âA third of
an inch of rain was added to the
storm's total last night and early this
morning, making a total of 1:20
Inches. This is a trifle above the nor.
mal for this period.
JOHNSON WOULD LIMIT
NUMBER OF DRAFTED MEN
President of American League Thinks
Big Teams Should Pay $1000
Each for Players.
CINCINNATI, Jan. 10.âThe Na
tional Baseball Commission at Its
meeting to-day considered the changes
in the National agreement desired by
the National Association of Minor
League Clubs. Several speeches were
made, most of the speakers urging
greater consideration for the minor
leagues. Ban Johnson said he was
opposed to the request of the minor
leagues regarding drafted players.
He offered a substitute, which has
been taken under advisement by tho
representatives of the National Asso
ciation of Minor Leagues.
Mr. Johnson proposed that each
club in the National and American
Leagues be limited to the drafting of
not more than four or five players
each year. He favored a maximum
price of $1000 for drafted men.

CROWD LINERS
Arrivals of Russian Hebrews
at New York Number
75,160 in Five Months
FLEE FROM CZAR'S LAND
Desire to Evade War Serv
ice Responsible for the
lnpour of Immigrants
NEW YORK, Jan. 10.âDuring the
last five months the number of Rus
sian Hebrews arriving at this port has
exceeded that of any other nationality,
and has amounted to 75,160. The im- â 
migration officials view the situation |
with alarm. In the ten days they have
Bent back by the steamships on which |
they came 528 immigrants, chiefly on
the ground that they were "assisted."
It is claimed that many societies
exist in Europe for the purpose of i
supplying fleeing Russian Hebrews
with tickets for the United States and
coaching the fugitives to answer the
questions of the immigration officials.
. To lessen this inpour of what the
officials of the Department of Tom
I merce and Labor regard as undesirable
I immigration the officials of Ellis Is
i land are preparing records of many I
"assisted" cases for submission to Con- |
gress. j
The flight of the Hebrews from Rus
sia is due to a desire to evade war
service.

EXPLAINS FALSE NAME.
Asked why he lived under an as

FAMED IS DUDE
OF THE ENGLISH
HOUSE OF LORDS
IyOONQ FAR!,. CONSIDERED THE
PEST PRESSED MAN IN THE
HOI SE OF lords.
fr r*
LONDON. Jan. 10. -Though he Is
only 32 years old. the Earl of Beau
rhamp is said to Dossess the distinction
i
of having made more speeches than .
any other living Englishman. There \
may be some exaggeration that, I
but it is certain that he is the most i
loquacious member of the aristocracy, i
He believes that it is his chief mission
in life to set <>ther people right by be- j
stowing upon them unlimited advice
and counsel. As it costs them nothing
for the most part they endure it good- ,
naturedly. He never lacks an opportu- !
nity to ventilate his ideas, because lords
are always in demand at public func- ,
tlons in England. It has not yet been >
noted that he has ever accomplished
anything by his oratory, but he is still
young and persevering.
The Earl is a very good-looking man.
and he knows it, too. Likewise he is
well aware that he has very shapely
hands, and they always figure promi
nently in his photographs. In his
younger days he was regarded as the
dude of the peerage and is still con
sidered the best dressed man in the
House of Lords. . .
A DEVOUT CHURCHMAN.
He is very devout and it was said
that in his early youth he had decided
to enter the ministry. He changed his ;
mind on that point on succeeding to !
the ancestral estates and titles when
only 19. He has, however, always
shown keen interest in church matters :
and only the other day he berated
church choirs and organists for being
more devoted to music than to religion
and thereby sacrificing piety to art.
When he was 27 years of age a Con
servative Government sent him to New |
South Wales as Governor of that col- j
ony. The Antipodeans, who have much j
less respect for hereditary titles than
English folk, did not take very kindly
to him. His state dances were de
risively dubbed â¢â¢Seidlitz Powder Balls"
because tickets of admission were blue
and' white. The holders of the blue
pasteboards were entitled to a better
supper than those who had been pro
vided only With white ones, and had
other exclusive privileges, too. To the
democratic colonists distinctions of
this sort savored of snobbishness and
they resented it.
FOE OF RACE SUICIDE.
The Earl did not mend matters any
hy taking them to task for their small
families and declining birth rate. That
sort of talk comes all very well from
President Roo-seveit, whose precepts are
backed up by the example his own
home furnishes of a large and healthy
family, but Australian husbands and
wives regarded it as sheer impertin
ence coming from a young peer, who
was at that time stili a bachelor, al
though he could well afford a wife.
They told him bluntly that before he
began to preach upon the obligations
of matrimony he should first get mar
ried himself, and intimated that there
were plenty of Australian girls who
were quite capable of sustaining the
title of Countess with grace and dignity
He did not profit by that hint.
It was long supposed that he would
never marry because, according to
tradition, a curse had been laid on the
family. It was directed against the
eldest son of an eldest son and de
clared that such a one would never in
herit the wealth of the family because
it had been derived from the spoils of
the church. The Earl is an eldest son,
but in 1902 he decided to risk the curse,
so far as any future heir might be in
volved, by marrying Lady Lettice
Grosvenor, a daughter of the late Earl
Grosvenor and sf sister of the second
Duke of Westminster. It was what is
called a brilliant match.

BISHOP TALBOT
MUST BE TRIED
Repudiation of Signatures
to His Presentment Does
Not Settle the Case
INQUIRY BOARD MEETS
Members Express Opinion
That Charges Stand De
spite Act of Vestrymen
READING, Pa., Jan. 10âDespite the
lack of a quorum, seven members of
the board of inquiry appointed to
probe the charges made against Bishop
Ethelbert Talbot of the Central Penn
sylvania diocese of the Episcopal
Church by L N. W. Irvine, the deposed
priest, met in this city to-day and,
after a three hours' conference, ad
journed to meet here on Friday. The
members of the committee Informally
considered the case in many of Its
phases, and, after its adjournment, it
was the consensus of opinion that ac
tion must be taken to save the good
name of the church, as well as In jus
tice to the accused prelate. The mem
bers of the board were of the opinion
that there could be no withdrawal of
the grave charges and the case ought
to be settled once and for all.
William D. Butler of Mauchchunk.
Pa., a layman of the board, who was
elected secretary, said that, not
withstanding that the Huntingdon
signers had repudiated the charges and
that the other signers had asked Bishop
Tuttle to permit them to withdraw the
accusations, the charges stood as prom
inently as ever before the members of
the board.
Butler is also authority for the state
ment that Bishop Tuttle has refused to
acquiesce in the request of the majority
lof the presenters for the withdrawal of
the charges.
"The charges once having been made
and signed," he said, "are no longer
. the property of the presenters, but are
j now the property of the church, and
: they have nothing to do but to let the
' matter take its own course."
All the members of the board and the
presenters agree in one paragraph of
the withdrawal paper, which read as
â¢ follows:
! "We earnestly desire that the long
existing scandal in the church shall be
I forever quieted."

PUNY ATTEMPT
TO MAR STATUE
Notoriety Seeking Person
Tries to Deface Kaiser's
Gift to United States
NO DAMAGE Ig DONE
Heroic Figure of Fred
erick the Great Unscathed
by a Mimic Explosion
Special Dispatch to The OUL
WASHINGTON, Jan. 10. âA sensa
tion was caused In this city to-day
by the discovery of what appeared to
be a definite and serious attempt to
blow up or deface the statute of Fred
erick the Great, given to the United
States by Emperor William of Ger
many and erected in the arsenal
grounds near the Army College on the
Potomac River water front. In the
Southern part of the city. It waa be
lieved at first that a crank had made
a real effort at destruction of the
statue, but after a careful investiga
tion the police officials say the ma
terial used was incapable of producing
damage. All the evidence so far ob
tained, the police announced to-night,
tcr.ded to show that the attempted
effort to injure the statue waa puny
and amateurish to a degree. It Is re
garded as an atempt to work up a
sensation.
Detectives learned that a negro
hackman named Arthur Carter took
the man thought to have perpetrated
the affair from the Pennsylvania Rail
road to the statue. Apparently he had
just arrived in the city. His baggage
consisted of a small and worn dress
suit case. The man told the hackman
to drive slowly to the barracks
grounds. When the statue was
reached the stranger alighted, ran up
the sfHps leading to the westerly ter
race of the War College esplanade and
disappeared behind the pedestal of the
statue. A minute later he ran back
to the cab and the hackman noticed
that the grip which the stranger had
carried was missing. The man jumped
into the cab and ordered the hackman
to drive out of the grounds as quickly
as possible. Soon afblr getting out of
the grounds the stranger jumped out,
paid the hackman and disappeared
tcward the river front.
Post Baker Reiger. at the barracks,
saw the cab drive up and the man
jump out and run toward the statue
with a satchel under his arm, from
which smoke was issuing, but he
could not see what became of the
man.
The man had hung the suit case by
a wire hook to the iron fence sur
rounding the statue. A few minutes
later a negro discovered it and dis
lodged it from the fence with a piece
of scantling. As it fell to the ground
it exploded. Aside from throwing a
few splotches of mud upon the steps
of the pedestal no harm was done.
One effect of the explosion was the
hurried building of a small watch box
beside the statue, where a watchman
will be stationed all the time here
after.

V> PJDNESDAY -~y_ LLLV. J â â ~ r'l^PA^LJhJ^ 9 }
OAKLAND had a clear sheet on which to write a municipal
water policy that would protect her people against bad ad- j
ministration, and insure a thoroughly business-like policy in |
acquiring and running a municipal plant. There has been progress
in the world in these matters, and there is an accumulation of ex
perience for the guidance of the future. It is settled in this country
and in Europe that no private plant should be left in the field to
compete with a public plant, and ttiat the cost of the public plant
should rest upon it alone, leaving the public credit free. It is also
settled that the organization of the municipal government should
have the official machinery required in the creation and operation
of a public plant, and that this official machinery should be per
manent, resting upon merit, and not subject to the political changes
of personnel, which are inevitable in a government resting on suf
frage. Our sister city seems to have turned her back on each of
these settled principles and, as might have been expected under such
circumstances, favors a proposition that is open to all of the objec
tions that may be brought against municipal ownership and to
others, more serious, that inhere in the specific plan adopted.
We regret that this noted California city has refused to make a
ikw datum line, from which all future experiments in municipal
trading would be measured. With such an opportunity to prove that
a municipal plant can be bought on its own credit and successfully
run and paid for out of its own revenues, leaving the public credit
free for use in the desirable public improvements which press upon
all modern cities, it is regrettable that Oakland should prefer to imi
tate the worst policies of the past, rather than take the honor of
originating the better policy of the future.
It will be noticed that a refusal to make a municipal water plant
carry its own bonds and its own cost of administration is a signifi
cant admission that municipal ownership is a failure. The propo
nents of that policy should 4>e glad of the chance to prove that its
administration can be as economical and as business-like as that of
a private plant. When they have the chance to prove this, and
refuse it, they cannot complain if the opponents of municipal social
ism take advantage of their refusal.
In the Oakland proposition are certain singular features that
may be examined independently of any views for or against munici
paf ownership. With the power to condemn the existing plant and
so eliminate its competition, it is proposed to bond the city for a
plant that is not yet in existence, and whose rights to any water
arc in litigation, and the city is denied the right to make any con
tract for that plant until after its hands are tied by voting the bonds.
That is to say, the people are asked to vote bonds, tie their hands,,
mortgage the public credit, leave a competitor in the field, without
having any contract for anything. As the plant for which the bonds
are to be voted was condemned by the only expert who has exam
ined it. and as the bonds are to be pledged in advance of any con
tract for the delivery of any specified quantity of water, and as the
Belters propose to use wood pipe instead of iron, the project seems to
be in discord to all business principles.
If our interest in such public questions and our friendly interest
in our neighboring city qualify us to advise, we would surely advise
the people of Oakland to insist upon a complete contract before they
vote on bonds, and to vote down all bonds until they have a con
tract and know for what they are voting. The expenditure of ap
proximately $6,000,000 without any contract would revolt any busi
ness man. No individual, firm or corporation would think of doing
such an unbusiness-like thing; then why should a city tamper with
the public credit to do it? We have seen no reason given either by
members of the city government or the sellers of the proposed plant
for refusing to make a contract before voting bonds.
The spirit if not the letter of the law for such bond issues is that
the people in voting on bonds vote to ratify or reject a contract. In
Oakland they are asked to vote millions to a project for which there
is no contract. We regard such a vote as void in law. Of course
the implication is that the proposed contract has features which the
people would not indorse, therefore these must be concealed. All
proper policy and all fair dealing require that the people shall have
the contract before them, to ratify or reject as they see fit. Instead
of this the people are asked to mortgage their property for millions
to carry out a contract which they are not permitted to see. Such
a proposition has in it the elements of all kinds of loss, scandal and
trouble, and it should be stopped now by making a contract and
letting the people see it and vote upon it.
If we cannot have the satisfaction of seeing a right start made
by basing the bonds on the plant they create, we can have the satis
faction of advising that at least the people, who are to pay the bill,
be permitted to see the contract on which they are voting.
A CORRESPONDENT finds analogy between retirement at
â  the age limit of 62 in the army and navy, and the retirement
of United States Senators at the same age. There is no anal
ogy. The age limit in the army and navy is not based upon the su
perannuation of officers, but is for the purpose of permitting the line
to move up by promotion. At 62 a man may be in his mental and
physical prime, indeed most men are. General Miles is as well qual
ified to lead an army as he was twenty years ago, and Terry, the
last rear admiral retired in the navy, would resent the insinuation
that he is not as good a man as ever. 4
In public life outside the army and navy we have abundant evi
dence of the great usefulness of men who are long past 62. Of the
Senators Morgan is 81, and his colleague is 84, and they both do
full duty. Of others past the age of 62 are Berry, Perkins, Bard,
Teller, Patterson, Piatt of Connecticut and Piatt of New York, Haw
ley, Bacon, Cullom, Allison, Blackburn, McCreery, McEnery, Hale,
Frye, Gorman, Burrows, Alger, Nelson, Money, Cockrell, Clark of
Montana, Gibson, Millard, Stewart, Gallinger, Dryden, DepeW,
Mitchell, Aldrich, Bate, Proctor, Dillingham, Daniel, Foster, Elkins,
Scott, Spooner and Quarles. So it appears that near half the Sena
tors are over the age at which our correspondent would retire them.
Allison, Cockrell, Morgan and the late Senator Hoar, the oldest
of all. were among the most useful men in either house of Congress.
To empty the Senate of these men on account of age would be to
deprive it of much of its best working material and the most useful
to the country. The saying "old men for counsel, young- men for
war," is justified by the world's experience, though it is not a denial
that young men may be also qualified for counsel, as we know they
are. But old men are not disqualified. We think it is wise that the
constitution fixes a minimum age for the Senate of 30 years and
for the House of 25, but omits a maximum entirely.

Roman and Arabic Numerals.
It takes three and one-third times
as long to write the Roman numerals
from 1 to 100 as the Arabic, and the
chance of error is twenty times as
great. It takes three times as long
to read the Roman numerals from 1
to 100 as the Arabic, and the chance
for error in this Is eight time* as
great Tet there Is a great advantage
in the Roman numerals, for nearly all
the clvlMxed world .knows them.

Big Factory Makes Hairpins.
The greatest of the world's manu
factories of hairpins is at Painswick.
a village situated in the Stroud Val
ley, at the foot of the Cotswolds. There
are no fewer than SOO persons, says
the Engineer of London, employed In
turning out these trifles of the bou
doir, and hundreds of automatic trans
chines are in constant operation trans
forming miles of wire into tons of
finished pins.

FIVE OF HIS
SONS KILLED
IN THE WAR
MICHAEL. ABRAMOFF.
+*- L +
THE accompanying portrait Is that
of Michael Abramoff, a Russian
knife grinder, who has received
an autograph letter from the Czarina.
He has fourteen children, nine of whom
went to the war. Two were killed and
one wounded at Liaoyang, while at
the Shaho battle three were killed,
fighting side by side, and three serious
ly wounded.

ficy, bridesmaids. Dr. Robert Burns

WANTS TO AID
THE PLANTERS
Washington Official Says
University Needs Improved
College of Agriculture
A semi-official letter bearing upon
the establishing of an agricultural col
lege at the University of California waa
received several days ago by Judge P.
J. Shields of Sacramento, and forward
ed by him to this city. It was yester
day read at a meeting of the State
Board of Regents.
The letter came from Dr. A. C. True,
director of the Office of Experiment
Stations, Department of Agriculture,
Washington, D. O, and was ah embodi
ment of his views on the establishment
of an agricultural college, the necessary
preparations to insure the endowment
of same by the Government, and the
practical good that would result to
California from such a step.
Dr. True told of the interest the de
partment -vas taking in agricultural
education at the California University
and outlined the Intentions of the Gov
ernment toward procuring a farm for
the College of Agriculture. This farm,
he maintained, should be in prox
imity to Berkeley, so as to avoid mak
ing it a separate institution. The course
should be broad-gauged and include
special studies in all branches of
farming.
In the meantime, Dr. True adds, Cal
ifornia must strengthen her agricultur
al work so as to be in a position to
conduct good college courses and turn
out the best class of students. The pres
ent agricultural building, he adds, is
wholly unsatisfactory. It lacks lecture
rooms, laboratories and equipment for
instruction. Some of the most impor
tant departments art quartered in the
attics, and some in the stables.
Dr. True then goes on to compare
the present agricultural quarters with
those of other branches and says that
poor accommodations keep students out
of the classes.
President Wheeler of the university
is in receipt of a letter from Professor
Campbell of the Lick Observatory, stat
ing that he had received $3000 from the
Carnegie Institute of Washington, to
be expended during the next two years
in measuring and reducing the Cross
ley reflector photograph plates of the
minor planet, Eros. The plates are of
the highest value in aiding to deter
mine the distance between the earth
and the sun. After the transaction of
routine business the meeting adjourned.

BIG BUSINESS
DEAL EFFECTED
Family Difficulties Cause De
la Montanya & Co. to
Part With Their Concern
The domestic difficulties of the De la
Montanyas have culminated in the re
tirement from business of their great
hardware concern. The doors of their
establishment at 214 Jackson street
were closed to the trade forever j*ea
terday noon. The property has been
purchased by the Pacific Hardware
and Steel Company for a sura approxi
mating half a million dollars. The big
deal was effected yesterday morning
after five days of preliminary negotia
tions. The -formal transfer of the stock
will take place at an early date and
all visible assets will be removed to
the gigantic new plant of the purchas
ing concern at Seventh and Townsend
streets.
Last Friday a representative of J. de
la Montanya A Co. visited H. J. Mor
ton of the Pacific Hardware and Steel
Company and offered to sell the Jack
son street establishment at any reason
able price.
The directors of the larger concern
took the matter under consideration,
looked over the property of the Mon
tanya firm, and then offered a ball
million dollars for the business, con
tingent upon the amount of stock is
hand. The offer was immediately ac
cepted, for the Montanyas earnestly
desire to retire from the local hardware
field.
The Jackson street concern has bees
in business for more than a quarter ol
a century. It was reckoned one of Ban
Francisco's strongest wholesale houses.
The Montanya family harmony has
been frequently shocked of late, how
ever, and the members of company de
cided that it would be for the interesti
of all the stockholders to give up the
battle in the business world. Morton
of the Pacific Hardware and Steel
Company says the Montanyas bad a
splendid business, much better than
he had supposed, in fact. Their trade
in stoves he said furnished a particu
larly agreeable surprise.

WILL SUPPORT
YOSEMITE BILL
oard of Trade Passes Reso
lutions Indorsing Federal
Ownership of the Park
STATESMEN TO LEND AID
Belshaw and Estudillo Are
Ready to Present Measure
in Senate and Assembly
The members of the State Board of
Trade held a session yesterday after
noon and quickly disposed of the Yo
semite Valley bill among other things.
When the report of the committee ap
pointed to investigate the matter was
read a motion was* made for its adop
tion and it was carried unanimously.
This means that the board will do all
in its power to turn the Yosemite Val
ley over to the Federal Government
and divorce it entirely from State in
fluence.
Some time ago a committee com
posed of William H. Mills, C. M.
Wooster, S. F. Booth and Arthur P.
Briggs was appointed by the board to
make a report on the proposed bill.
The quartet worked earnestly on the
subject and received the support of
several prominent organizations in va
rious parts of the State, including the
Sierra Club, which has taken an active
interest in the flght for a long time.
The committee reported that Sen
ator C. M. Belshaw and Assemblyman
Miguel Estudillo of Riverside had vol
unteered to make a vigorous cam
paign In'both houses for the passage
of the bill. These two statesmen
some time ago came to the rescue of
the Sierra Club and promised to aid
that body to have the bill put
through.
Comments on the measure were
made by Chairman Briggs, Colonel
Irish, William H. Mills and C. M.
Wooster. They all agreed that the
fight for the passage of the bill will
be a bitter one and that the opposing
faction is leaving nothing undone to
retain the property for the State.
A copy of the report by the com
mittee, together with a copy of the
bill and other data pertaining to the
Yosemite, was read and approved and
Secretary Briggs was ordered to have
a copy sent to the Legislature and
Senate at once. It is expected that the
bill will come up for a hearing in a
short time and the board ts sanguine
of success in winning its.flght.
A. W. Van der Naillen sent a com
munication to the board informing it
of the proposed exhibition to be held
at Leeds, Belgium, and requesting
that the body make an effort to rep
resent California products there. Af
ter a lengthy discussion it was agreed
to support the measure provided the
fruit growers raise sufficient funds to
carry the matter through.
A telegram from Senator Perkins,
telling of his work In aid of the Oak
land harbor, was read. Perkins is at
present trying to pass a bill providing
for an appropriation to better the har
bor across the bay. The Water and
Forest Association requested the
board to lend its aid in securing an
appropriation for certain surveys. The
board refused to act in this matter.
Manitoba Tourists.
A carload of 30 tourists arrived in the
city yesterday direct from Winnipeg.
Manitoba, via the Northern Pacific and
Southern Pacific, en route to Southern
California for the Winter.
Yesterday was spent in sight-seeing
around San Francisco and the party left
for the South last evening.
They are to be followed by a second
party which the Northern Pacific Com
pany has scheduled to reach here the
28th inst.
TRIAL OF TAYLOR WILL
CONTEST IS UNDER WAY
Half-Sister, Who Was Not Remem
bered, Seeks to Set Aside
Old Man's Testament.
A beginning was made in Judge
Troutt's court yesterday in the trial
of the contest of the will of the late
James D. Taylor, but because the lead
ing witness was without her glasses
and could not identify certain docu
ments an adjournment was taken until
this morning. The witness was Mrs.
Ella H. Tajbott, in whose house Taylor
died and who witnessed the will. She
is charged by Taylor's half-sister, Llz
sie Taylor McÃride, with having un
duly influenced the old man in the
making of his last testament, which
was drawn three days before hft died
in April of last year. The contestant
alleges that Taylor was suffering from
Injuries received in an accident and
was not of sound mind. Mrs. Talbott,
sh<Â» declares, asked him to make a will
and herself had it prepared, getting
the names of his relatives by examin
ing letters he had received from them.
In this process the sister was omit
ted and the $20,000 estate was left in
equal shares to a brother, nephew and
n'ece in Wisconsin and a niece, Bee
Taylor, In Washington, D. C, with
$[.00 added in a codicil for a nephew
in Missouri.
Bee Taylor achieved some notoristy
some time ago by losing a Government
position at Washington because she
criticised the President's Filipino
policy.

NEWHALL AGAIN
IN THE CHAIR
President and Officers of
the Chamber of Commerce
Named at Annual Election
RESOLUTIONS ADOPTED
Building of New Tug for Use I
of Revenue Service at;
This Harbor Js Advocated.
The annual election of the San Fran- !
clsco Chamber of Commerce took place
yesterday afternoon. George A. New
hall was re-elected president, C. H.
JBentley and William H. Marston were
elected vice presidents and W. J. Dut
ton, William L. Oerstle, George D.
Gray, Kufus P. Jennings, Charles 8.
Laumeister, William Matson, H. B. |
Morton, Thomas Rickard, James Rolph
Jr., James B. Smith and William R,
Wheeler were elected trustees. The new
members of the board of trustees are
Messrs. Gray, Laumeister. Morton and
Matson.
The new board of trustees will not ;
begin their duties until after the next
annual meeting of the chamber, which
will take place next Tuesday. The old
board of trustees after the election of
yesterday, which was unanimous,
transacted some business. s
Fred Holmes & Co. was" elected to
membership.
A bill favored by the California Wa
ter and Forest Association to ap
propriate $76,0tu) for the Joint work of
the United States and the State of Cal
ifornia in the lines of utilizing water ;
resources and forest preservation was
l&dnvsed. A resolution was adopted
advocating the building of a new tug
for the use of the revenue service at
9m Francisco. Action was taken in J
accordance with a request from Phoe- |
nix and Maricopa County, Arizona, to ,
protest against the Joining of New ;
Mexico and Ailtona as one State. A 1
resolution from the pioneer women of i
California favoring a legislative appro
priation for placing two statues of
eminent Californians in the National
Capitol at Washington was adopted.
A resolution was also favored re
questing that all supplies for the Pan
ama canal shall be carried in ships
flying the American flag. The alumni
nurses asked for indorsement of their
move for a charter amendment to pro
vide that the management of the hos
pitals conducted by the city and county
of San Francisco shall be non-partisan.
The trustees took favorable action.
MOTHER'S COUSIN WANTS
TO GUARD BABY SMITH
Third Application for Custody of In
fant Heir Filed in Court by
Robert Peterson.
A third applicant for the guardtan
ship of two-year-old Randolph Wil
son Smith, son of the late Butler
Smith, appeared yesterday in Robert
Peterson, who says that he is a cousin
of the child's deceased mother, Ella
Roberta Smith. Peterson also asks
for the guardianship of the boy's sis- j
ter, Roberta. The second Mrs. Smith
left a fortune of $100,000 in trust for j
her children, in which the father had
a life interest. The third wife, Emily |
May Smith, who married the real es- j
tate dealer a month before his death,
now has the children and has applied
for their legal custody. Another ap- j
plication for guardianship of the boy
was made by Mrs. M. Findlay, his
nurse.
Peterson, in his petition, sets forth
the situation regarding the fortune
and says that the stepmother's inter
ests are in conflict with those of the
children and therefore she is not the
proper person to be their guardian.
He says that he and his brother and
sister are their only blood relatives.
The sister, Mrs. Elizabeth Willis, lives
at Napa.
The applications of Mrs. Smith and
Mrs. Findlay were to have been heard
yesterday, but were continued until
Friday.

WINNER TO BE DECLARED TO-DAY IN FIELD TRIALS
YOUNG DOGS DISPLAY RARE
FORM, SURPRISING OWNERS
ASSIIETON SMYTH
Special Dispatch to Tb< Call.
BAKERSFIELD, Jan. 10. âThe Pa
cific Const Field Trials Derby is still
unfinished, but a winner will be de
clared to-morrow before noon. A
start will then be made In all-age
stakes. The young dogs showed great
form to-day and the veterans in at
tendance say they never saw so many
high class animals started in one
stake.. Ideal conditions prevail for
The development of good work, as
birds are plentiful and the weather is
.cool, with light winds.
The sportsmen here are enthusiastic
over the work shown by the setters
and pointers. Their ranks were
largely increased to-day. The morn-
Jug's trains brought men from bothi
the north and south. There is a fine
spirit of sportsmanship shown in the
field, no sign of jealousy nor of com
mercialism being in evidence. No
one seems to give a thought to the
prizes, while all join in praising any
clever work shown by a dog.
The first brace of dogs put down
in the morning were W. W. Van
Arsdale's black, white and tan Eng
lish setter Mariposa, handled by C. H.
Babcock, and J. E. Terry's black,
white and tan English setter Ken
wood, handled by Fred Coutts. They
proved a brace of fast, wide ranging,
merry working dogs, with Mariposa
slightly the better in all these
teristics.
MARIPOSA IN GOOD FORM.
After several wide casts had been
made Mariposa dropped to a point in
high weeds, where conditions seemed
unfavorable. In these weeds each dog
secured two points. Mariposa was at
once marked by the knowing ones
as sure to go in the second series. The
next brace down was Rose and
tensen's black, white and tan Eng
lish setter Del Rey Belle, handled by
Charles Coutts, and M. Lawrence's
lemon and white English setter
Youley, handled by S. M. Carlisle.
This brace was in good cover, where
there were plenty of birds, and they
scored' a number of points. Youley
was somewhat deficient In style.
After these came Fargo Rose's
orange and white setter Rival, handled
by W. B. Coutts, and W. B. Coutts*
liver and white pointer Bolins Duke,
handled by Charles Coutts. These had
a long run, which was not productive
of much point work. While both
at times showed fairly good speed and
made some clever casts, they were un
certain in their work.
J. B. Terry's lemon and white Eng
lish setter Ladylike, handled by W. B.
Coutts, and A. Cadigan's liver and
white pointer dog Chiquite, handled by
J. E. Lucas, were the next brace down,
and they gave the spectators a clever
exhibition of speed, wide casting and
clever point work, Ladylike scoring 6
points to the dog's 4. From the char
acter of the ground over which they
worked and the high class of both
dogs possibly no heat in the race. up
to this time was better appreciated by
the assembled sportsmen.
. Mountain View Kennels' liver and
white pointer Sandango, handled by J.
E. Lucas, was cast off to run his bye
on the same ground when the last
brace was taken up. Soon after he
was put In action, in attempting to go
through a wire fence, he injured his
fore leg, which lamed him so serious
ly as to destroy any chance he might
have had for the stake.
COMPETITION IS CLOSE.
The officials and spectators f*hen
moved to a new field, about a mile dis
tant, where Judge Betten announced
that the following dogs would take
part in the second series: Nellie P
with Sir Roderick, Ladylike with Mari
posa, Del Rey Belle with Eurbank
and Chirjuite with Japan. The first
brace of the second series was Nellie
P and Sir Roderick. Both dogs went
away at a clipping gait, Sir Roderick
making one cast into the timber which
kept him out of sight so long as would

EXPERT PICKS
ALL-STAR TEAM
Seven Stanford Men, Three
Berkeleyites and One Ne
vada Kicker on the 801 l
â¢
Special Dispatch to The Call.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Jan. 10.
In the December number of the Stan
ford Alumnus Archie Rice, Stanford
'93, who has been a close follower of
Western football for the last ten years,
has picked an "All-Pacific Coast elev
en." Stanford is given seven places on
the team, California three, and Nevada
one.
The personnel of the all-star aggre
gation is as follows: Left end, Clark
(170), Stanford; left tackle, Sprott (178),
Stanford; left guard, Thompson (190),
Stanford; center, Stroud (195), Califor
nia; right guard, Roosevelt (190), Stan
ford; left tackle, Heitmuller (185), Cali
fornia; left end, Snediger (173), Califor
nia; quarterback, Stott (150), Stanford;
left half, Chalmers (175), Stanford;
right half, Weiler (195), Stanford; full
back, Freisell (175), Nevada.
The only surprises on the team is the
placing of Snedigar at end and Freisell
at fullback. Rice says the latter has
no peer at the position, placing him
ahead cf Weller of Stanford, who is
shifted to halfback.
Rice praises the w r ork of this year's
Stanford eleven, declaring it the best
team the Cardinal has yet turned out.
MRS. CLARK IS WINNER
OF DRIVING CONTEST
Mrs. Leroy Nickel Captures Prize in
Approach-Putting Competition
on Presidio Links.
The ladies of the San Francisco Golf
and Country Club held competitions in
driving and approach-putting on the
Presidio course yesterday morning.
Each competitor drove Aye balls, the
aggregate distances driven being as
follows: Mrs. J. R. Clark, 551 yards;
Mrs. Chesebrough, 448 yards; Mrs. R.
D. Girvin, 412 yards; Mrs. W. G. Mil
ler, 290 yards; Miss Ives, 197 yards;
Miss Guittard, no returns; Miss Car
roll, no returns.
In the approach-putting conf est each
lady holed out six balls, three from
a distance of Afty yards and three
from a distance of seventy-Aye yards,
over a net. Mrs. Leroy Nickel's score
was 18, Mrs. W. G. Miller's 19, Miss
Guittard's 20, Mrs. R. D. Girvin's 20,
Miss Ives* 21, Miss Chesebrough's 22,
Miss Carroll's 22 and Mrs. J. R.
Clark's 24.
have appeared a bolt had he not on
the previous day made several casts
of the same character to his handler.
A number of points were secured In
the latter part of the-heat, a majority
of which were to Sir Roderick's credit.
The work of both dogs was good, but
the heat resulted in the latter's favor.
The next and last brace of the day
was Mariposa and Ladylike. This
proved another great exhibition of
fast, wide ranting and classy work on
the part of both dogs. While Lady-
I like had given an excellent exhibition
jof point work in her preceding trial,
| Mariposa outclassed her in that respect
lin this trial. Some of the casts made
â¢by this brace and the bird sense
I shown in covering their ground are
I rarely surpassed, even by aged dogs.

to-night resulted as follows:

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL. WEDNESDAY, JANUARY It, 1905.
TRAVERS PILOTS VENATOR
THROUGH IN THE HANDICAP
Martinmas, Favorite
in Betting, Takes
the; Place. .
Public Choices All
Meet Defeat at
Oakland.
BY FRED E. MUEHOLLAND.
A heavy fog the night before and
the peek-a-boo tactics of the sun found
the track at Oakland yesterday still in
a very muddy condition.
It .'aught the form students up in the
air, and as six favorites failed to toe
thf scratch it can easily be surmised
that the bookmakers carried off the
swap. Prickles, Melar. Barney Drey
fuss and Stilicho furnished the sur
prises. Fine weather attracted a large
crowd.
While Venator, with 112 pounds,
]"<>kpd to have a royal chance of cap
turing the six and a half furlong handi
cap, the bookies insisted on installing
Martinmas a pronounced favorite. The
Henshall entry was also asked to jug
gle 112 pounds, which he failed to do.
Travers soon had Venator in front af
ter the start and Dr. Rowell's sprinter
won easily, without ever being headed
or extended. The favorite experienced
no difficulty in subduing the feather
weight. Sea Voyage, securing place
honors.
OUTSIDER ROMPS IX FIRST.
Walter Jennings" filly, Princess
W'heeier, does not find the path lead
ing from the maiden class strewn with
roses. Ridden by Jakie Jones, she had
a decided call in the betting on the
opening event, and finished second.
Prickles, an S to 1 chance, with Bonner
up, made a runaway race of it, winning
without effort.* Macene, one of the
Ezell entries, ran a close third. ,
There looked to be only one chance
of downing Del Carina, the 7 to 10 favor
ite for the sprint following, and that
was to have the filly left at the pOst.
Sam Greenfield did not get her left,
but he rode a race that would do credit
to a bronco buster, and was defeated
by Melar, a 9 to 1 chance, piloted by-
Tommy Clark. Fay Templeton was
third. Entre Nous, the second choice,
ran out in front to the stretch, then
faded away.
BERENDOS BADLY BEATEN.
Some few of the layers chalked up
5 to 1 against Lady Kent when betting
began on the six-furlong selling run.
but not many pasteboards were passed
out at that quotation. At that, the
mare closed an 18 to 5 second choice
and, ridden by Greenfield, won in hol
low fashion from Dr. Sherman. Albe
marle secured the show. Helgesen got
away poorly with Berendos.Mhe favor
ite, and was never a contender.
"Pacing Dick" De Neffe, who Is aa
liable to turn up in Indian Territory
as he is at a White House dinner, finally
put one over. His Dorian gelding, Bar
ney Dreyfuss, dropped from the clouds
in the mile selling Axture.leading Major
Tenny over the line nearly a length.
Isabellita, the 11 to 5 choice, ran a fair
third. The winner went to the post at
odds of 6 to L
Brinkworth & Co.'s Little Wally
bumped up against it again in the clos
ing mile and three-sixteenth selling
event. Byronerdale, the 17 to 10 favor
ite, ran an exceedingly poor race, but
Stllicho, a 6 to 1 chance, displayed ex
ceptional speed, beating Little Wally
very cleverly by nearly a length. Modi
cum, a well touted and played starter,
finished third.
TRACK NOTES.
The card at Oakland this afternoon
is far above the average. Sir Wilfred
and Daruma meet again in the two
ytar-old event, and in the last race of
the day Sad Sam, The Lady Rohesia,
W. R. Condon and other fast ones
come together.
Sam Greenfield piloted two winners.
Both Tommy Clark and O. Chandler
are riding well. Chandler has improved
greatly over his Portland and Seattle
form.
George Berry, superintendent of
Napa Stock Farm, was a visitor at the
track yesterday. George is carrying a
handsome gold split second chronome
ter presented to him by A. B. Spreckels,
proprietor of the big Napa breeding es
tablishment, as a memento of the win
of Daruma, first of the get of Imp.
Solitaire to sport the colors.
Elrodun, the property of Lagoria &
Forbes, was sold In the paddock before
the first race to A. Libenthal for $850.
At a meeting held yesterday by the
directors of the California Jockey Club,
the incumbent officials were re-elected
for the ensuing year.
The stewards of the Pacific Jockey
Club, the governing turf body of the
coast, also met yesterday. The dates
recently claimed by the Seattle and
Portland associations were ratified, as
were the licenses issued by the Los
Angeles Jockey Club. Harry Stover,
owner of Kenilworth Park, Petaluma,
asked for 28 days, but was given only
12, to follow after the close of Oak
land on May 6.
The Knapp case was also taken up by
the stewards, but no decision rendered.
A copy of the contract purported to be
held on the services of "Big Bill"
Knapp by Puerl Wilkerson was shown.
The original contract it was claimed
by Wilkerson is now on the way here
by mail from New York, and until it is
produced nothing further will be done
In the matter.
It is said Green Morris, who is racing
a strlns of horses at Ascot Park, re
cently refused an offer of $10,000 for
Gold Rose, the crack three-year-old Al
ly.
To date races have been divided
among the winning horses at Oakland
as follows: %
Winner of 1 race 98
Winners of 2 races 49
Winners of 3 races 24
Winners of 4 races 3
Winners of 5 races 3
Winners of 6 races 1
Sad Sam, Pelham and Honiton have
each captured Aye events. Bell Reed
holds the record with six to his credit:*

CORRIGAN MAY BE BARRED
FROM SEVERAL RACE TRACKS
Fails to Make List of Entries and
Trouble Is Brewing at
Memphis.
MEMPHIS, Term., Jan. 10.âBe
cause Edward Corrigan failed to make
his usual list of entries to the Mem
phis Jockey Club stakes, which re
cently closed on account of his actibn
in declaring all colts and fillies out of
the local Derby and Oaks, horsemen
here say that he will be seen compet
ing at few tracks this year under
jurisdiction of the Western Jockey
Club. A rumor that the local jockey
club has practically sounded open
war to be waged against Corrigan in
behalf of the Western Jockey Club by
refusing to accept entries would not
be discussed by the members of the
Memphis Jockey Club or its officials.
CORBETT SIGNS
TO BOX NELSON
Denver Lad and the Great
Bane Will Meet in Re
turn Battle Next Month
The long expected return match be
tween Young Corbett and Battling
Nelson was framed up last- night. Mor
ris Levy, manager of the Hayes Val
ley Athletic Club, announced that the
boys had signed to box twenty rounds
under the auspices of his organiza
tion in February at 130 pounds at 6
o'clock, the same weight at which they
battled before.
The match has been under considera
tion for a long time past and it was
no secret that Levy had planned to
bring the lads together in a return
bout. Corbett has been crying for a
chance to redeem himself ever since
the night that the Battling Dane beat
him to a pulp and now that the op
portunity has been given him, his
friends say he will go to work and
get ready for the meeting.
The minor details of the match have
not as yet been settled. It is likely
that some discussion will occur when
the referee question is about to be ad
justed. Nelson says he will have no
more of Roche, though he had no
cause for complaint, as the Bowery
Boy acted right when the Dane was
beaten by Britt.
Corbett likes Roche good enough to
let him judge any of his fights and
will surely propose his name. He is
also friendly with Eddie Graney again
and when their little quarrel was
patched up the Denver boy stated that
Graney was the man he would hold
out for the next time he was due to
battle with someone.
. Nelson is at present away In the
woods on an extended hunting trip
and will not return for a couple of
weeks at least. His tramps through
the mountains will keep him in good
form and a fortnight of training in
the evm will be all he needs to make
him fit and ready, as the weight is
easy for the champion of Hedgwich.
Corbett is working at Shannon's, San
Rafael, and says he is getting into
shape. He had said this so often that
the public does not always take It
with the best of grace. This time
the former champion will have to show
the sports. He Is possessed of a great
chance to make good and It is up to
him.
The winner of the battle will un
doubtedly be signed up with Britt un
less Mr. Herford beats the Californlan
to it and frames up one for his black
terror. Herford ls> still on the look
out for a match and announces his
readiness to take on any of them.
SANCHEZ CONVICTED.âJuan Sanchez, a
Filipino youth, was convicted by a Jury yes
terday in the United States District Court of
stealing a watch and a pair of shoes from
the Presidio headquarters. He was ordered to
appear for sentence this morning.

CLAUDE AND DAINTY ARE
BILLED IN THE BROOKLYN
Only Six Horses Now Racing in Cali
fornia Entered to Compete for
Rich Prize.
NEW YORK, Jan. 10.âWith the
arrival of to-day's mails from Cali
fornia the final entries were received
for the Brooklyn Handicap. Fred
Echberger, secretary of the Brooklyn
Jockey Club, received only six entries
from the West for the great event,
which brought the total number In
this race up to forty-nine. The en
tries to-day were M. J. Daly's iron
horse Claude, which has won many
good races on California tracks this
winter; James McLaughlin's Pasa
dena, W. B. Jennings' Dainty and
Proper, J. L. Holland's Martinet and
U. Z. Armand's Sir Brillar.

Yachtsmen to Choose Nominators.
The members of the San Francisco
Tacht Club will hold their regular
meeting in the banqueting hall of the
California Hotel to-night for the pur
pose of electing three members of a
nominating committee. The three
members chosen will nominate candi
dates for the various offices of the
club, the names on the list prepared
by them constituting the "regular
ticket."
TALENTED YOUNG PIANIST
IN IRISH MUSIC RECITAL
Miss Mary Carrick Will Render Selec
tions Of Erin's Masters To
morrow Night.
Miss Mary Carrick. the talented
young San Francisco pianist, will give
a recital of old Irish melodies to-mor
row evening at Steinway Hall, on Sut
ter street. This will be Miss Car
rick's second public recital. She is
a pupil of Hugo Mansfeldt and ap
peared in a concert under his direc
tion more than a year ago and made
a success.
Miss Carrick's recital to-morrow
night will be something new in the
musical line, for the programme of
Irish melodies which she is to play
has never been rendered in this city
before. The programme is a varied
one and embraces a long list of the
most popular compositions from the
pens of the most noted works of Ire
land's renowned writers of music and
song. Following is the programme in
full:
"Come Back to Erin," op. 210 (Kuhe); "The
Minstrel Boy," op. 29 (Pape); â¢Believe Me If
All Those Endearing Young Charms," op. 26
(Pape): "Love's Young Dream," op. 38 (Pape);
"The Last Ross of Summer" (Thalberg-Cun
neen), for the left hand alone; Fsntasle on
Irish Airs (Kuhe); "The Harp That OneÂ©
Through Tara's Halls," op. 32 (Pape); "Kath
leen Mavourneeu," op. 72 (Kuhe); "Home,
Sweet Home" (Thaiberg), by request.
BIG WRESTLER
BEATS CARKEEK
The Veteran Cannot Eesist
Strength of Tom Jenkins,
Who Has Youth in Favor
CELEBRITIES PRESENT
Crowd Listens to Prize Fight
ers, Bull Fighters and
T. McGrath's Noisy Vest
By sheer strength Tom Jenkins, the
champion all-round wrestler, forced
Jack Carkeek, the veteran of catch-as
catch-can, to the mat twice last night
at Woodward's Pavilion. It was Father
Time who helped get the victory for
the big fellow. The elder man showed
that he had all the tricks of the game
by heart, but he could not work his
holds en Jenkins.
The veteran went down fighting on
each fall. Every muscle on the frame
that was not as supple as It used to
be strained to the utmost in futile re
sistance as the Cleveland giant forced
his shoulders to the mat.
The first fall was won in 17 minutes
and 30 seconds. Carkeek was thrown,
but cleverly stalled all attempts of Jen
kins to get a good hold. Juat when it
looked like curtains for the veteran he
suddenly slipped away and put the big
fellow on the defensive, but not for
long. Jenkins secured a half Nelson and
bar wrist hold on the veteran. One of
Carkeek's shoulders touched the mat.
Jenkins threw into the hold all his
enormous strength. Slowly the other
shoulder of the veteran was forced
down. Carkeek struggled gamely, but
youth told. The other shoulder touched
the mat. Referee Eugene Van Court
tapped the big fellow in the back. First
fall for Jenkins.
The second fall resulted after some
lively work. Carkeek caught the big
fellow napping and had him guessing
for a few minutes. The veteran se
cured good holds twice. If he had the
ginger of other days, Jenkins would
still be wondering how it happened. But
Jenkins escaped the slaughter and got
a three-tiuarter Nelson. Suddenly he
shifted to a half Nelson and an arm
hold. Jenkins fhrew forward all hlÂ»
weight and Carkeek was out. Time, 4
minutes and 45 seconds.
A preliminary bout between Al Brill
and George Braun went to Braun.
George Harding refereed this match.
Though Brill was heavier, Braun han
dled him easily, taking the first fall in
8 minutes and the second In 5 minutes
and 36 seconds.
All the celebrities were there. Billy
Jordan got a compound fracture of the
voice trying to introduce them. Young
Corbett was the first to be called before
the calcium vest of Tim McGrath. The
little fellow was welcomed like a long
lost brother.
Then entered J. Edward Britt, boy
orator of the ring. "I am ready to
fight any man in the world at 133 ring
side," announced "Chames."
Like a shot Young Corbett leaped
back into the ring, "I'll fight Britt at
that weight any time with a side bet
of $5000, winner to take all," he an
nounced.
With the hauteur of pate de foie
gras in his demeanor J. Edward strode
back: "Ladies and gentlemen," he
announced, "I have defeated Mr. Cor
bett once. I do not think I should
fight him again until I have tried all
the new material. I can announce
that Uaere will be one more fight be
twesjHfeSngland and America. I am
negotiations to fight Mr. Ja
bez Wnlte of England."
Mistah Jonsing also presented
to the clamoring multitude temporari
ly eclipsing the splendor of Tim Mc-
Grath's vest. Kid McCoy, accom
panied by a swagger cane, was an
nounced as the "Beau Brummel of the
prize ring." Frankie Neil and Dick
Hyland, who are to mix soon, also
made their little bows.
The sensation among the introduc
tions was Senor Henrico Robles of
Madrid, Spanish bullfighter by occu
pation. "I no speaka the Engleep,"
announced the Don. "But I flght-a da
bull. I wrestle any bull in America
for 500 pesos. Take him by the horn
and throw him. Fine and dandy, you
call him, what?" and the toreador
from Spain smiled a smile that was
all good nature. The bullfighter was
in tow of Young Corbett and company.
Spider Kelly, the cotillion kid, and
T. McGrath Esp., assisted by a vest,
second Jenkins. Mr. McGrath an
nounced that the poem of haberdash
ery was worn in compliment to the
ladies present. The presence of the
fair sex caused Billy Jordan to ad
monish the gentlemen present to use
parlor language and to say, "Oh,
mercy!" or "Gracious," if excitement
overcame them. The admonition was
heeded.
Butchers' Board of Trade.
At the last meeting of the Butchers'
Board of Trade the following officers
were elected for the ensuing year:
President, Captain J. H. McMenomy;
first vice president. Fred Winters;
second vice president, J. W. Phillips;
treasurer, L. Nonnemann; recording
secretary, B. J. Horn; financial secre
tary, D. A. Huntemann; marshal, J.
Nowlan; assistant marshal, Henry
Meyn.
