APPLIANCE CO., 6 6'rirttU gtreitUNCLE SAM'S GREAT DREDGER CHINOOK
WILL BE REPAIRED AT THIS PORT.
.vrâ
The contract for repairing Uncle
Sam's big dredger Chinook, formerly
the army transport Grant, was award
ed'yesterday to the Risdon Iron Works
of this city. The Chinook is now at
T'acoma, and every engineering firm of
â¢consequence on the coast was after the
â¢work. The repairs to be made are ex
t'ensjive, and the Risdon's bid of $21,310
â¢ was, the lowest.
' The Chinook is equipped with much
complicated machinery. In converting
her to. a dredger the latest theories of
-construction were put into practice. To
; a - certain extent the Chinook was a
success, but in the practical work not
ât*. few of the ideas which had governed
.her- construction were knocked on the
'licad. Some of the mistakes made in
\he original plans were responsible, it
â¢is'said, for the extensive repairs now
necessary. The wear and tear on the
machinery during the time sha was in
â¢commission are said to have been out
;of all proportion to the work the
â¢Chinook did. When the repairs are ef
fected it is expected that the Chinook
â¢â¢#â¢111 be an invaluable aid in making
[navigable waters now too shoal for
â¢ safety In anything but fine weather.
' Following were the bids for repair
ing and overhauling the Chinook: Ris
.'don Iron Works, $21,310; Union Iron
â¢Works.' $22,252; Puget Sound Iron
.Works. Seattle, $22,333; Willamette Iron
Work's, Portland. $26,349; Heffernan

SAILORS STRIKE
DURING A GALE
Refuse to Save the Lakme!
Unless Guaranteed More
Pay From Vessel's Owners
CREW MUTINIES ON BAR
'Captain Carlson Forced to
Use Much Diplomacy in!
Order to Rescue His Ship
A strike of sailors when the vessel on
which they were employed was in im
minent danger of destruction was a
novel phase of the labor question
brought to light yesterday during the
investigation into the disabling of the
steam schooner Lakme. On January
1, shortly after 8 o'clock in the morn
ing, as the Lakme was crossing Hum
boldt bar, she struck the south spit
of black spar buoy No. 1, losing her
rudder and disabling her engines. The
vessel listed heavily and lost a portion
of her deck load by the heavy seas that
dashed over her, causing her stout tim
bers to quiver as though she were
about to go to pieces. At this junc
ture Captain John VV. Carlson, master
of the Lakme, ordered the crew to
jettison some of the remaining deck
load in order to right the vessel. To
his astonishment and indignation the
sailors went on strike. They refused
to lift a hand unless the captain would
agree to pay them overtime. Captain
Carlson, by the exercise of diplomacy
and cajolery, succeeded in inducing
them to save the vessel and to end the
strike.
The captain told Unifed States Local
Inspectors Bolles and Bulger that all
the crew were foreigners and that there
was not an American among them.
The situation becoming very critical,
the crew were transferred to the tug
Ranger, which attempted to tow the
disabled Lakme into port, but the haw
ser parted. Captain Carlson ordered
the crew to go off in the small boats,
recover the hawser and make fast.
They mutinied again and refused to
perform this duty. Finally, with the
assistance of the Ranger and the
steamer North Fork, the Lakme was.
towed into this port badly damaged.
NORWEGIAN SOCIETY WILL
HOLD SINGING FESTIVAL
First Anniversary will Be Celebrated
at Turn Vcrcin Hall Next
1 , Saturday.
The Norwegian Singing Society of >
, Bar. Francisco will celebrate its first
anniversary since its reorganization on
| Saturday evening, January 14, at Turn
Vercin Hall, 353 Turk street.
Frequent and spirited rehearsals
! have been held by the society, under
j the direction of Professor S. Lintner,
!in anticipation of the occasion, and
in consequence thereof an excellent
' rendition of the many numbers can
.be expected. Ther will be two instru- j
' mental selections, a piano solo by Mrs. j
i Klitgaard and a violin solo by Mr. <
j Hartwig.
The Swedish Singing Society has
j volunteered to lend its assistance
I for the evening, and the combined
I choruses of the two societies should
i help to make up a programme far I
; more interesting than those given [
'. heretofore by any of the Scandinavian j
; singing societies.
| It is the aim of the society to arouse
and Deroetuate the interest of their
j countrymen in the songs and music of I
the fatherland, which have in the past
been sadly neglected.
The following committees have the
' affair in hand:
Reception committeeâKlaus Olsen. |
chairman; Otto Ottersen and Anton
Lochin.
Floor committee âW. Jensen, man
! ager; B. Halversen, assistant.
FUGITIVE FROM JUSTICE
IN HANDS OF POLICE
Andrew Jackson Smith. Alias James
Guinn, Says He Is Wanted in Ver
mont for Burglary.
A middle-aged man who said his
name was Andrew Jackson Smith sur
prised Sergeant Brown at police head
quarters yesterday by informing him
that he was a fugitive from justice
and desired to surrender himself. The
sergeant took him into Captain Bur
nett, to whom the man told his story.
Smith said he had been convicted of
burglary in Woodstock. Vt., in 1885,
and sentenced to serve fifteen years in
the State's prison at Windsor. In 1887
he was transferred to an asylum at
Brattleboro, from which he made his
escape ten months later. He came to
this State and about four years ago
he was arrested in Madera, Montery
County, for burglary. He was
sentenced to serve four years in San
Quentin under the name of James
Guion and was discharged last month.
The records were examined and
Smith's statement as to serving four
years in San Quentin was verified.
Smith was taken to the City Prison
and the authorities at Brattleboro,
Vt., were notified by wire of his sur
render.
Steamship Men Incorporate.
Articles of incorporation were filed
yesterday by the Steamship Associa
tion of San Francisco, which an
nounces its purpose to promote and
protect ail interests connected with
American shipping on the Pacific
Coast; to maintain general headquar
ters; to provide a place and means for
just, fair, convenient and economic
employment of employes and to ad
vocate legislation for the betterment
of the industry. The capital stock of
the association is $100,000 and the in
corporators are Robert Dollar of San
Rafael, W. G. Tibbetts of Alameda, E.
A. Christenson of San Francisco,
George D. Gray of Oakland, J. R.
Hanify of Sausalito, C. R. Johnson of
Fort Bragg and Robert H. Swayne of
Alameda.
Shares in Whaler's Profits.
In Judge Hunt's court yesterday, a
jury awarded Philip D. Montgomery
judgment for $1500 against Horace P.
Smith as his rightful share In the
profits of trading with natives in the
far, north on a whaling cruise six years
ago. Smith was captain of a vessel
sent out by the Pacific Steam Whaling
Company and Montgomery was the
maÂ£e. They had an agreement, as
they had on other cruises, to divide
the profits of their enterprise. The
cruise in question was. more profitable
than usual and the captain, who was
paid $3000, pocketed the whole
amount. The defendant is now in the
Arctic.

EPPINGER GRAIN
FOR THE BANKS
Trial of Suit to Determine
Manner of Apportionment
Begins Before Murasky
WAREHOUSE CONFUSION
Diagrams and Evidence Of
fered to Show Impossibil
ity of Identifying Lots
The little pile of grainâ3soo tonsâin
the warehouse at Crockett, which was
all that the banks had to show for their
warehouse receipts for 37,500 tons of
wheat and barley given as security by
the Eppingers for loans of more than
a half million dollars, is now at issue
in Judge Murasky's court. True, the
grain has been sold, under an order of
court, and the $102,000 received for it
remains to be distributed among the
creditors, but there is disagreement as
to the manner of its apportionment.
The outcome of the present suit is
expected to determine whether it shall
be prorated among the banks in pro
portion to their claims, or given to
Henry Wadsworth, the Eppingers'
trustee in bankruptcy, for the benefit of
all the creditors of the firm, or given to
the banks according to the specific lots
on hand which were described in the
warehouse receipts. For instance,
Wells-Fargo's bank claimed 1000 tons
of the 3500 tons that remained, and
specified certain lots in the warehouse.
This would leave little for the dozen
other local and rural banks which
loaned their funds so lavishly, and it
is contended that it is imposlble to
identify the separate lots of grain de
scribed by the receipts because they
were moved from warehouse to ware
house, from gangway to gangway, and
new numbers were substituted for old
ones until all were commingled and
confused.
The suit is that of the Nevada Na
tional Bank against the other banks,
Eppinger & Â£0., the Pacific Warehouse
Company arm Henry Wadsworth, the
trustee. A dozen leading members of
the bar are participating and the case
for the plaintiff was presented by At
torney Frank H. Powers. The Nevada [
Bank's claim was for $49,500. It sued
for a determination of the respective !
rights.
The general sideaof the case was put !
forth yesterdayâthe fact that there
was a warehouse and that it contained
separate lots of grain variously desig
nated. This was explained with the â 
maps and diagrams used in the crim- I
inal trial of the Eppingers, and the cvi- I
dence of Qeorge W. Russell, who pre- j
pared them. Frank S. Mitchell, book- I
keeper for the Eppingers, under in- j
structions of the court, had prepared a
list of the various lots and their mark
tags and his testimony thereupon was
taken.
The trial of the case may last for !
three weeks and will be resumed to- i
day.
Complains He Was Robbed.
John Larkin, 308 Leavenworth I
street, was found asleep in a doorway j
on Harrison street, near Third, short
ly after midnight yesterday morning |
by Sergeant McGovern and Policeman
Small. They awakened him and he
walked away toward Fourth street, f*
About an hour later he met Police
men Clancv and Small and told them
he had fallen asleep in a doorway and
had been robbed of a purse contain- i
ing $140. He had no recollection of J
being awakened by the sergeant and
Small. He said the last thing he re- !
membered was drinking in a saloon j
at Kearny street and Montgomery aye- !
nue with "Jack" O'Connell, a baseball j
umpire.
Geber's Trial Proceeding.
The jury to try the case of August :
Geber, charged with the murder of i
Charles Hartmann, at 321 Third 1
street, on July 10, was completed in I
Judge Dunne's court yesterday morn- !
ing. Assistant' District Attorney j
Whiting made the opening statement,
and the trial proceeded. The wit
nesses examined were Corporal George
Russeli of the Police Department, Dr.
John J. Kingwell. George Hartmann*
and Susannah Hartmann, father and
mother of the murdered man, and P.
Ploner. Their evidence was the same
as at the preliminary examination.
Geber seems to take no interest what
ever in the proceedings.

Masked Man Robs Soldier.
William Thompson, a soldier, re
ported to the police last night that he
was held up and robbed by a masked
man at the- corner of Montgomery
avenue and Vallejo street shortly
after midnight. The highwayman re
lieved Thompson of $7 and made good
his escape.
Dragged By a Car.
George Hughes, a clerk residing at
1110 Eddy street, tried to board an
Eddy street car near Mason late last
night. He missed his footing and was
dragged several feet, sustaining a dis
location of the left leg.
POLICE BOARD
SEATS POHEIM
Commissioner Begins Work
by Favoring Renewal of
the Restaurant Licenses
WEEK'S GRACE IS GIVEN
Resorts Must Make Changes
to Comply With Law or
Permits Will Be Refused
Without any special ceremony Dr.
Joseph F. Poheim, appointed to the
Board of Police Commissioners by the
Mayor, presented his credentials last
night at the regular meeting and at
once began his official duties. The
election of a board president was not
referred to during the evening and
will be decided at a later session.
The proprietors of three French
restaurants, the Old Poodle Dog, Del
monico's and the Bay State, appeared
last night before the Board of Police
Commissioners to ask for a renewal
of their licenses to retail liquor in
their places of business. The licenses
of all three places expired during the
past two weeks and the board has re
fused renewals unless the proprietors
comply with the municipal ordinance
prohibiting the sale of liquor to rooms
over restaurants.
j Nathan M. Adler, proprietor of the
I Bay state, asked for a continuance of
â  one week to enable him to make ar
i rangements with his landlord to ef
! feet changes so as to comply with the
I ordinance. On a tie vote the applica
, tion was denied as far as concerns the
serving of liquor to the rooms. He
was given permission to run the res
taurant downstairs for one week
more.
Adler appealed from the board's de
cision and solicited the support of
Commissioners Drinkhouse and Po
heim. They were in favor of grant
ing the week's extension for the en
tire establishment. Commissioners
Hutton and Reagan steadfastly voted
against allowing liquor to be sold In
the rooms.
j Commissioner Drinkhouse charac
terized the refusal as equivalent to
taking the places away from the pro
! prietors. Commissioner Hutton re-
I torted that It was not a question of
j morality but of the law and that at
I present the places were being con
j ducted in an unlawful manner.
Delmonico's, at 112 O'Farrell street.
I was refused a renewal, but was grant
ed a week's time to make alterations
jto comply with the law and file a new
I application. The board took the same
! action on the license of the Old Poodle
j Dog. A citation was issued to revoke
(the license of the Oberon, 38 O'Farrell
i street.
Patrolman James S. Cook of the
Southern station was found guilty of
not responding to an alarm of fire on
the morning of January 2. He was
fined $25.
Robert Hosman, who was before
the board last week charged with
obtaining money under false pretenses
In connection with his running an in
telligence office at 61 Third street, re
quesMfl the board to transfer his license
to a Miss Rogers, his secretary. The
Commissioners held that the transfer
was no guarantee that the business
would be conducted legally and voted
to revoke the license.
The Grand Jury sent a communica
tion to the Police Commissioners ask
ing them to postpone the hearing of
charges of corruption Tn Chinatown
against Sergeants Ellis, Fraher and
Brophy and Patrolmen Doran, Heinz,
O'Brien, McGrath and Munn. The
hearing was accordingly postponed
until next Wednesday evening.
At midnight the board went Into
executive session to discuss the French
restaurant question and to review the
regulations of the department in rela
tion thereto.
INSANE PATIENT FIGHTS
WITH HOSPITAL ATTENDANTS
His Suddenly Acquired Strength Made
It Necessary for Several Men
to Subdue Him.
With suddenly acquired almost
superhuman strength an Insane pa
tient struggled with all of the ferocity
of a wild animal in an effort to gain his
freedom yesterday afternoon in the
Detention Hospital at the City Hall.
The man is David Curry. He made
an attack on Steward Mannville, who
was escorting him to the detention
ward. The maniac was strangling the
attendant when Edward H. Riis, a re
porter, came to his assistance. Riis
dragged the madman from the stew
ard and the lunatic turned upon him.
A rough and tumble fight followed.
Riis suddenly received a stunning
blow in the face which staggered him.
Assistance came in the persons of
Steward Devere, Ambulance Driver
Rix, Chief Steward Charles Beecher
and Chief Matron Mrs. Floyd Cannon.
The tussle was further enlivened by
the arrival of two policemen. Curry
was conquered and strapped to a bed,
not, however, until he had left marks
upon those who tried to subdue him.
Chief Matron Cannon received a blow
on the left wrist which will cause her
to carry her arm in a sling for a few
weeks, while Riis is nursing a con
tused and lacerated lip. All of the
others have cuts, bruises and contu
sions.
When the unfortunate man came to
his senses shortly after the struggle
he regretted what he had done and
gave an account of himself In a clear
and intelligent way. He said he is
a native of British Columbia and had
been in Arizona, where he was con
fined in a lunatic asylum but was dis
charged as cured. Last Sunday, while
at work in San Mateo, he felt his in
sane spell returning and at once has
tened to this city, where he told his
story to Dr. Carl G. Wilson at the
Emergency Hospital.
ELECTRIC CONTACT MELTS
RINGS ON LIEB'S FINGER
Caterer of the St. Francis Hotel Meets
With Painful Accident After
a Banauet.
Caterer Lieb of the St. Francis Ho
tel met with a peculiar and painful
accident Monday night that has re
sulted in his right hand being placed
on the disabled list. A wedding feast
had just come to a close on the mez
zanine floor of the hotel when Lieb
opened the door of an electric switch
board located in the corridor and pro
ceeded to turn off the electric lights in
the room in which the banquet had
been held. His little finger was
adorned with two plain gold rings
that came In contact with a live wire.
Suddenly there was- a loud report and
Lieb was knocked backward by a ter
rific shock.
Recovering, he glanced at his hand
to discover that the two gold rings
had been melted together and were
sizzling in the burning flesh of his
finger. A physician was summoned
and Anally succeeded in removing the
gold bands and dressing the disfigured
digit of the caterer, who will not en-
Joy the use of his right hand for some
time.
STOCK RAISERS
INVITED HERE
Indications That the Next
Session of the Association
Will Be Held in This City
ACTIVE CAMPAIGN ON
Eight Over Part the Pack
ers and Railroads Shall
Play in Their Deliberations
c 41
special Dispatch to The Call.
DENVER, Jan. 10.â"Come to Cali
fornia and have a good time. Prune
your appetites under our prune trees.
Make dates under a date palm. Go
out to the Cliff House and yell at the
seals. Hop through Chinatown and
take tea with the Japs. Let the Juices
of our vineyards warm the tendrils of
your hearts, and if you don't go 'nut
ty' over the California climate under
our walnut trees, I miss my guess."
Such arguments as these, coming from
C. W. Coe of San Jose, are winning the
fight of San Francisco for the next
convention of the livestock men. Coe
also is giving to everyone who vlslta
the California headquarters a package
of prunes. The California delegation
is almost sure to ~et the next conven
tion. The Los Angeles contingent,
which had been credited with having
an eye on the gathering, is to Join
hands with the San Franciscans.
Chicago and Kansas City were cred
ited with wanting the convention of
1906, but diligent inquiry among the
delegates from these cities failed to
reveal any one who was boosting for
them. Fort Worth and Dallas, Texas,
would like to secure the gathering,
but it is unlikely that they can mus
ter enough support.
A hot fight has broken out in the
ranks of the National Livestock As
sociation over the question of whether
the railroads and packers are to have
representation on the governing board
of the association.
President Frank J. Hagenbarth iÂ«
in favor of the plan and has urged
it strongly. There is a heavy conting
ent of stockmen, particularly those
from the West, who are opposed to' thÂ»
idea, and they are snaking a strong
fight to keep the management of the
organization solely in the hands of the
men who are actively engaged in the
handling or care of stock.
The committee to which the matter
was referred met as a whole to-night
and will report to-morrow to tne gen
eral convention.
The probability is that the -ailroada
will be denied representation on the
Board of Control of the Livestock As
sociation. The general committee of
the livestock industry could not reach
an agreement to-night and will meet
again in the morning. The feeling
manifested against the admission of
the railroads was very strong and the
discussion grew so animated that it
was thought best to adjourn without
action. The packers and all other al
lied industries will probably be ad
mitted, and the railroads alone left
out.
With 1000 delegates in attendance
the annual meeting of the National
Livestock Association was called to
order this morning by President F. J.
Hagenbarth. After the president's ad
dress, John W. Springer of Denver, for
eight years president of the Livestock
Association, spoke in memory of the
members of the association who had
died.
The convention then took up the
question of reorganization and wae
busily engaged with it for several
hours.
The convention of the National Wool
Growers' Association was held this
afternoon and all the old officers and
members of the executive committee
were re-elected. A meeting of the
Western Cattlemen was also held in
the afternoon and a number of papers
were read on the problem of con
trolling cattle scabies.

Th>'" high winds caused no damage.

CHICAGO BOARD OP TRADE.
Future Grain and Provisions.
CHICAGO, Jan. 10.âThe whent market at 1
the opening gave evidence of some willing
ness to sell below the figures current at the
end of the previous day's session. Initial
quotations on May were unchanged to %c
lower at $1 17 to $1 17%. The factor that
Inspired the early selling was a decline In
prices at Liverpool. Aside from the weak
ness of foreign grain markets, however,
the tenor of general news was bullish.
Northwestern receipts were small and the
extreme cold weather In the Northwest prom
ised a continuance of light movement from
the farms. The visible supply statistics
showed a decrease of 353.000 bushels. As a
result bearish sentiment in the pit soon gave
way and the market became remarkably
strong. Shorts were anxious buyers and
quickly bid up the price of May to $l 18%, a j
price only a trifle below the high point
touched during the speculative furor due to
the Northwestern crop calamity. Later in.
the day all of tho advance was lost and
prices dropped to a point considerably below
yesterday's closing. May selling off to $1 16%.
The break was due largely to heavy realiz
ing sales by a leader of the bull clique.
There was also moderate selling resulting
from a partial let-up In the demand for cash
wheat at Minneapolis. The market closed
rather weak, %c lower, with May at $1 16%.
The corn market was comparatively Arm in
spite of lower prices at Liverpool. May
closed at 44% c, a shade higher.
Oats were dull. May closed %c higher at
31 %c.
Provisions held fairly steady in the face
of heavy offerings. May pork closed 5c off,
lard was down 2%@5c, and ribs were 2%c"
lower. J

Wheat and Other Grains.
WHEAT FREIGHTS âThe demoralization
seems complete. The best rate now obtain
able for European business is said to be 12s 6d.
which is ridiculously low and no spot char
ters are reported. The chartered Wheat fleet
in port has a registered tonnage of 6845,
against 7300 tons on the same date last year;
disengaged, 36,780. against 110,000; on the way
to this port. 177,435 tons, against 188,600.
Mall advices from Sydney, under date of
December 12. say: Wheat chartering for Byd
. ney loading' has ceased, as It Is now very
I certain that tha yield will be seriously short
iof expectation formed on outlook a month
1 ago. Harvesting is now fairly general, the
j results being. In many cases, most disappoint
ing. Millers are scrambling for new crop
grain at prices much about export basis. We
shall not be surprised if some charterers seek
to cancel a portion of their fixtures, while
we look upon a decline in freight rates from
Australian ports as inevitable.
This condition will probably stop the move
ment of vessels from this port to Australia,
seeking business, and is an Important factor in
the current decline In rates.
WHEATâForeign futures were weak. Chi
cago was fractionally lower. Most of the wires
being prostrated, there was no news of any
consequence beyond the bare quotations from
the East. This market was unchanged as to
the cash grain, but lower as to futures, with
May active and December quiet.

Beans and Seeds.
Beans are showing more life and tone. The
demand Is now beginning to Improve, though
slowly, and holders are asking higher prices
all around, as will be seen by the new quota
tions. Large and Small Whites are especially
firm and the latter are reported scarce. Llmas
alone are weak and quiet, being affected by
the recent rains in the southern districts.
There is nothing new in Seeds.

Potatoes, Onions and Vegetables.
The Potato market is active and firm, with
fancy Burbanks passing out of receivers'
hands as fast as they come lh. There Is quite
an accumulation of low-grade river Burbanks
on the market and they are selling slowly.
There were no fresh Sweets received yesterday
and old stock ruled firm at the previously
quoted rates. Receipts of Onions have been
light for some time and stocks have been re
duced considerably. The best lots readily
brought an advance yesterday.
Offerings of southern vegetables consisted
chiefly of carrled-over stock, fresh receipts
being light, as usual on Tuesday. Choice Egg
Plant was scarce and higher and Peas did
better. The Mexican Tomatoes that came In
on the preceding day were still In receivers'
hands, the price. $1 75 per crate, being more
than the trade cared to pay.

Butter, Cheese and Eesrs.
The Butter market continues dull, with most
dealers complaining of an oversupply and will,
ing to meet buyers half way. Quotations,

more marked, and all houses are anxious to
get the goods off their floors. All. grades of
Eastern are practically cleaned up and are
therefore no longer quotable.

Hides, Tallow. Wool and Hops.
The Hop market has again taken a turn for
the better, and 1904 goods are quoted higher,
with more activity. The old quotations for
1905 contracts are continued, but there is
nothing doing at them, as buyers say it is too
early in the season to operate in them.
Wool quotations are dropped, as the market
is cleaned up of everything and the new clip
is not expected to appear before the middle
of March.
Sound Hides continue In active demand for
local tanning account, but grubby lots meet
with little attention. The market rules firm at
previous prices.

STOCK MARKET.
Heavy Sales of Gas and Electric on
the Bond Exchange.
The feature of the day on the Bond Ex
change was the very heavy business In Gas
and Electric. The stock, which glosed on
Monday at $49 50 bid. opened at $50, fell to
$49 and closed at $50 50, the sales amounting
to 2167 shares. Hawaiian Commercial sold'
up to $74 50 and there were sales of Bank of
California at $423 50 and California Jockey
Club at $111.
Business in all classes of mining stocks was
large and the boards were lively. The Com
stocks were higher all around and the Tono
paha were also Arm. The oil stocks continued
quiet.

COMMISSIONERS
ASSUME OFFICE
Aigeltinger and Egan Take
Seats on Works Board, and
.Maestretti Is President
VACANCIES NOT FILLED
W. H. Metson and Dr. F. J.
Tillman May Be Named on
Health and Park Bodies
The newly appointed Commissioners
erf Public Works Edward H. Aigeltinger
and Thomas F. Egan took their oaths
of oftice before the Mayor yesterday
and received their commissions after
filing their official bonds. Egan, who
had positively declared that he would
not accept the appointment because he
had been given Herbert Schmitz's un
expired term of one year instead of the
three-year term, evidently realized that
one-third of a loaf is better than none,
for after another conversation with the
Mayor he decided to change his mind
about refusing and signified his ac
ceptance of the office.
Aigeltinger and Egan at once re
paired to the meeting room of the
Board of Works, where Commissioner
Maestretti and retiring ommissioner
Schmitz were on hand to meet them.
The new Commissioners took their seats
and listened to a short speech from
Schmitz. in which he eulogized his for
mer colleague, Commissioner Maestret
ti, who was at once elected president of
the board, being placed in nomination
for the honorary position by Aigel
tinger. The beard then adjourned until
the regular meeting to-day. Both Aig
eltinger and Egan filed official bonds
with personal sureties.
The Mayor did not announce his se
lections yesterday for the vacancies on
the Park and Health boards to suc
ceed Frank Sullivan and Dr. Poheim,
now Police Commissioner. The place
on the Health Board has been offered
to Dr. Frank J. Tillman, who was de
posed from his position as assistant in
the laboratory by the very board upon
Which the Mayor desires to place him.
Tillman has indicated that he
â  will accept. He is a personal
friend of the Mayor and on that ac
â¢ count and because he more nearly rep
resents Ihe new school of medicine has
he been decided upon for the appoint
ment. William H. Metson is still men
tioned for the Park Commissionership.
"Doc" Leahy was offered the place, but
declined and made a special request of
â¢ the Mayor that Metson be appointed.
There are three secretaryships of
commissions to which new appoint
ments will t)e made in a few days.
They are those of the Park, Public
Works and Police boards. The scheme
, as outlined includes the appointment of
James de Succa as storekeeper of the.
Board of Education to succeed Thomas
Egan and the naming of Herbert
Schmitz to be secretary of the Park
Commission. There are several aspir
ants for secretary of the Police Board,
now that Alexander O'Grady has de
clared that he does not want it. Among
th.se is Barney Flood, an assistant
bond and warrant clerk, who is a
strong candidate for the place.
GRAND JURY TAKES UP
ELECTION BOARD CASE
Commissioners to Be Accused Again in
Connection With Appointments
of Primary Officers.
The ElecUon Commissioners are not
yet clear of Grand Jury entangle
ments. Although the charges pre
ferred by the former inquisitorial
body were dismissed by Judge Law
lor, the prosecution is to be renewed.
The present Grand Jury yesterday
took up the inquiry of appointment of
election officers for the primaries held
In August last, and it is thought the
accusation of malfeasance, demanding
the removal of the board, will be pre
sented at the next meeting, which is
to be held on Friday. It has been
generally known that the jurors would
file new accusations to correct the
technical defects of the previous
bills.
The Grand Jury also spent some
time yesterday in considering the mat
ter of police corruption in China
town. The investigation has been at
a standstill for some time. Police
Commissioners Reagan and Drink
house, in a conference with Fore
man Andrews, agreed not to take up
their trial of the accused police
officers until the Grand Jury shall
}iave concluded its inquiry.

STILL FIGHTING
TO BREAK WILL
Legal Battle for Alexander
Dunsmuir's Millions Is
Being Vigorously Waged
QUOTES AUTHOEITIES
J. C. Campbell, Attorney for
Mrs. Edna Wallace Hopper,
Makes Lengthy Arguments
The legal fight before Judge Cof
fey over the will of the late Alexander
Dunsmuir is growing warm. As on
the first day of the hearing, J. C.
Campbell, attorney for Edna Wallace
Hopper, was yesterday the central fig
ure at both the morning and after
noon session of the court. His argu
ment was based on the contention
that the will had not been probated
according to the laws of this State,
and hence had no standing in the
courts.
The law of the State of California
was very plain upon the subject of the
probating of a domestic will, he said,
and it was equally clear upon the pro
bating of a foreign will.
Mr. Campbell stated that Alexan
der Dunsmuir's home was San Fran
cisco. The will particularly stated
this to be a fact. To be in full ac
cord with the law the will should
have been probated in San Francisco
County and in this State. The wit
nesses to the signature to the will and
those interested in the will should
have been cited to appear at the time
the document was probated. None
of these conditions were compiled
with.
TAKEN TO VICTORIA.
The will was taken up to Victoria,
said the attorney, hundreds of miles
away from here, out of the State and
out of this country, and there It was
presented for probate by the chief
beneficiary, James Dunsmuir. At the
time it was probated there neither
one of the two witnesses was there
to testify. After these proceedings
were gone through with an exempli
fied copy of the document was brought
here and offered for probate. Neither
of the witnesses was cited to appear.
The court had no jurisdiction to pro
bate the copy of the will under these
circumstances.
"There was," said Mr. Campbell,
"no such thing as a will under the
common law. It is a statutory pro
vision and must be governed entirely
by the statutes."
* "Suppose the wiU in British Co
lumbia should be set aside? What
law would cover the result?" he
asked. "Would it come under the
statutes of succession of California
or the statutes of succession of British
Columbia?"
POINT IN CASE.
The main point which Mr. Camp
bell wished to bring out was that the
court here had no jurisdiction to pro
bate a copy of a will filed in a foreign
country by a resident of this county
and this State. Our statutes are plain.
The law is mandatory.
C. S. Wheeler, attorney for James
Dunsmuir, would occasionally ask Mr.
Campbell a question, but otherwise
Mr. Campbell had the floor to himself.
He cited authorities to sustain his arÂ»
gument. The case will be resumed
this morning at 11 o'clock before
Judge Coffey, when Mr. Campbell will
conclude his argument.
The same brilliant array of legal
talent that has graced the proceedings
since the trial began was on hand and
listened attentively to Mr. Camp
bell's arguments.
The hearing of the testimony, which
was to have taken place before Con
sul Bennett at 10:30 a. m. to-day, is
postponed.
Palo Alto Masons Install.
The following have been installed
as the officers of Palo Alto Lodge, F.
and A. M., for the ensuing term, the
ceremony being held in Fraternity
Hall, Palo Alto, on the first Saturday
in January. Josiah W. Roller, the
retiring master, was the installing of
ficer. Past Master M. Week acting as
master of ceremonies. The ceremony
and presentation of a jewel to the re
tiring worshipful master was followed
by a banquet and speechmaking.
Those who will control the lodge dur
ing the current Masonic year are:
Charles W. Decker, worshipful mas
ter; Lewis H. Barker, senior warden;
Milton B. Roller, junior warden; John
F. Byxbee, treasurer; William C.
Werry, secretary; David M. Crabtree,
chaplain; Aleck S. Ferguson, mar

BROKERS PROTEST.âCustom-house bro
kers yesterday forwarded to United States
Senator Perkins a protest against the plans
submitted for the'new Custom-house. They
complain that the offices where the greater
part of their business Is done are scattered

praiser's building Is too narrow.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, JANUARY 11, 1905.
SOUBRETTES ENGAGE AUTO
AND FAIL TO SETTLE BILL
Spend Two Days in Machine
. and Tour Two Counties.
Art-Admiring Saloon-Keeper Pays
$ 100 and Artists Go Free.
They were introduced to Police Judge
Cabaniss as "The Daudette Sisters,
Vallejo and Benicia," and their attor
ney vouchsafed the further infdrma
tion that they were professional vaude
villians, performing "a double turn"
at one of toe cheap theaters. Their ap
pearance did not belie that statement.
Petite, flashily attired and saucy-faced,
they were the soubrettes of the illus
trated sporting weeklies âthe nocturnal
consumers of cold bottles and warm
birds paid for by paretic old men and
sap-headed young ones.
It was for consuming $100 worth of
automobiling in a single dose and fall
ing to pay for the same that Vallejo
and Benicia were before his Honor
Cabaniss. The complaining chauffeur
| testified in bitter tone that they Jointly
; hired his machine and jointly occupied
I it, except during brief visits to way
! side cafes, while he whirled them to
I San Jose and over two-thirds of Santa
! Clara County's area and back to San
! Francisco, the round journey consum
! ing almost two days. When he depos
i ited them at the place of beginning,
Ellis and Powell streets, they gracious
: ly thanked him for giving them one of
; the nicest times they ever had and ex
| pressed regret that they were unable
) to prove their gratitude in a pecuniary
> way. Horror-stricken, he summoned
j Patrolman "Dave" Murphy and gave
! them into custody, apparently much to
their surprise and undoubtedly much to
their indigation.
"Ye don't mean to say, kid, that ye're
gettin' us pinched?" exclaimed Vallejo,
who, by the way, is called "Valley" for
short. \
"For Gawd's sake, officer, ye ain't
a-goin' to rush us to jail in th' hurry
up wagon?" was the expostulation ut
tered by Benicia, who is addressed as
"Bennie" by the bird-and-bottle buyers
hereinbefore alluded to.
Then they threatened to "throw a fit"
right there and then if the indignity of
I being conveyed to prison in a patrol
I wagon was insisted on, and to avert
j such an indelicate double turn, also to
! get his prisoners away from the midst
lof a rapidly accumulating and unani
! mously sympathetic crowd, the police
j man bundled them Into the auto and
i thus they were hurried to the Central
station.
"If our parents in Spokane knew we
were in such' a fix as this they'd die
right there," quoth Bennie to her custo
dian.
"Naw, they wouldn't do nothin' of
the kind," put in Valley; "they'd Jist
tap the ticker an' send us $10,000 to
pay our fine âthat's what they'd do."
"Folks rich, eh?" queried the officer.
"They've nothin but dough," was
Bennies affirmative, and Valley said:
"Aw, naw; they ain't rich. They're only
wealthy, that's all. Say, kid, they have
money enough to buy a million mer
chines like this'n we're ridin' in."
The desk sergeant discovered that
about six months ago the Daudette sis
ters were accused-of vagrancy before
Judge Fritz, who dismissed them on
condition that they return to Spokane.
They did so, but came a few weeks ago
under engagement as song-and-dance
artists.
While the Judge was wondering what
he would better do with the young
women the chauffeur was approached
by one Mr. Beauregard, a Sixth-street
saloon-keeper,who generously offered to
pay him the $100 demanded if he would
decline to prosecute. The offer was
promptly accepted and the defendants
discharged.
Mr. Beauregard insisted on having it
understood that his intercession was
prompted purely by desire to aid art in
distress.

I of the liquefied courtesies received from

thelender's knowledge.

Horace Angel operated a camera by

John's dance hall, on Pacific street, by
night until a policeman, being un
aware of his daylight vocation, arrest
ed him for wearing fashionable rai
ment and having no visible means of
obtaining it honestly.
Nervously toying with a budding
blond mustache, Mr. Angel proved
â  himself a fairly industrious profes
| sional photographer to the satisfac
> tion of Judge Mogan, and his explana

j temple of Terpsichore hlB permanent
| dwelling place was also accepted as
logical and earned his dismissal. So
JAMES C. CRAWFORD
passionately fond was he of dancing
and dance music, he said, that his
evenings were divided between indulg
ence in the exhilarating exercise and
absorption of the melodious strains
that wafted to his room from Portu
guese John's orchestra, consisting of a
piano, a banjo and an accordeon. Be
sides, long practlqe on the waxed
floor had made him such an adept in
all kinds of dances, from the stately
minuet to the more modern "rag,"
that his services were frequent
ly in demand to complete a "set," and
appreciation of his value in that re
spect induced Portuguese John to
charge him a very low room rental, so
as to have him convenient when he was
wanted to "All in." Of course his
modest income from the taking of
snapshots and the developing of nega
tives would not suffice to defray the
cost of his dancing if he were obliged
to pay regular rates for it. Any
lady or gentleman connected with Por
tuguese John's establishment could
and would be pleased to corroborate
every word he had said.
But his unsupported word sufficed.

Mrs. Rita Pratt emphatically de
clared that she was entitled to retain
possession of the garments which Miss
Paula Carter sought to recover via
the search-warrant route.
"After all I have done for that girl
this is a nice way to be treated by
her," sail Mrs. Pratt to Judge Mogan.
"My husband and I took her into our
home, at 725 Ellis street, and did ev
erything to make her feel as if she
was a member of our family. We took
her in to keep her away from bad
company, as we had known her par
ents, who were very respectable peo
ple, but after staying with us two
weeks she went off on her own hook
again. I'm keeping her things because
she's not trying to do right. A horse
man has her in tow. After treating
me so meanly I just wanted to show
her that I, too, could be mean, .and
you may as well tell her that until
she pays me $5 for the room and
board I gave her she won't get that
tailor-made and that wrapper and
that silk skirt and that shirt waist
and that pair of gloves and that box
of handkerchiefs and that lingerie."
Miss Carter did not appear to push
her complaint against Mrs. Pratt, and
the Judge continued the case until
the young woman finds it convenient
to visit the courtroom.
â¢ * â¢
Mrs. Margaret Murray of 16 Priest
street professed inability to understand
the sense of Justice that prompted
Judge Mogan to aid and abet her ar
rest and arraignment on the charge of
having battered the head of her 17
--year-old daughter Rose with a kitchen
poker, thereby inflicting a deep scalp
wound and several minor abrasions. If
the law, Mrs. Murray opined, proposes
to prevent maternal discipline of a
revolutionary child, then the law would
better be abolished and society permit
ted to obey its individual dictates as
to what is right and what is wrong.
Things have come to a pretty pass, in
deed, the lady declared, when any chit
of a girl can go before a magistrate
and effect her mother's arrest for ad
ministering corporal punishment. Had
the magistrate she then was address
ing ever heard of an adage relative to
thfe sparing of rods and the spoiling of
children? If not, he would better con
sult his Solomon.
The court bore meekly the rebuke
and as meekly hearkened to the oral
painting of Mrs. Murray's character in
rather somber colors by several of her
neighbors, who had been summoned as
witnesses for the prosecution. They
testified that she was a common scold
and much given to inebriety; that her
husband was forced to abandon her be
cause of her behavior; that her chil
dren were marvels of goodness, con
sidering their home life, and that a
term ln prison would not be a bad
thing for her or anybody connected
with her.
Then the defendant was ordered into
custody as the result of her having
tried to intimidate the complainant by
threatening to accuse her of wayward
ness and request that she be placed in
a reformatory institution. The girl â¢
testified that, she was employed as a
domestic and contributed $10 of her
monthly earnings to her mother, whose
display of ill-temper and incidental
poker nourishing were provoked by her
inability to obtain more money.
The mother will be sentenced to-day.
For practicing medicine without a
license on Seventh street Julian Waller
was fined $100 by Judge Cabaniss.

MAY ESTABLISH
MEMBERS' HOME
Delegates of Bricklayers'
and Masons' Convention
Discuss the New Project
MISSING TRUNKS FOUND
Philadelphia Union Is Pre
sented With a Testimonial
for Hospitality Extended
The proposition to establish a home
i for aged members of their union, that
j was brought up at the previous con
| vention of the Bricklayers' and Ma
j son's International Union at Trenton,
I Pa., was discussed again at yester
â¢ day's session of that body at Pioneer
! Hall. Delegate James Sullivan talked
;on the project, which will be finally
I settled before the adjournment in this
city. A corporation has asked the
international body for the privilege of
raising funds for the establishment of
| such a home, which is expected to be
I self-supporting.
The missing trunks of the delegates
j were found yesterday and prevented
J a great deal of inconvenience, as many
of them contained documents to be
read before the convention. The trunks
had been at the depot for a couple of
days. It seems the routing had been
changed and they came over the Sun
set route.
A number of resolutions were intro
duced in reference to rules. They were
referred to the proper committees.
Miss Emma Lanphere of Chicago, gen
eral organizer of the Retail Clerks'
i Association, and Mr. O'Dell were
granted permission to address the con
vention to-day.
A beautiful testimonial was present
ed to Philadelphia Union No. 1 by the
I convention as a token of appreciation
) of their hospitality to the delegates of
I the last convention at Trenton. The
{ testimonial is a cleverly made arch
I designed by Otto Helrich, a member
of Boston Union.
The presentation speech was made
by President William J. Bowen. R, H.
Kelly in behalf of the Philadelphia
Union responded with an appropriate
speech.
A meeting; of those interested in
State conference legislation was held
at recess to agree on some uniform
plan for conference work to present
to the convention. This subject will
be one of the important topics of the
meeting.
The delegates visited Chinatown last
night and this evening a high jinks
and smoker will be given at Scottish
Hall.

ABSCONDS WITH
UNION'S FUNDS
Warrant Issued for Arrest
of Maurice Donahue, Trus
tee of the Cooks' Society
For nearly a month the members of
Cooks* Union No. 44 have been aware
of the fact that one of their trustees
had absconded with $1230 of the
funds of the union, but for reasons
best known to themselves they have
tried to keep the matter from the pub
lic. The fact became known yesterday,
when Charles A. H. Smith, business
agent of the union, with headquarters
at 107 Mason street, swore to a war
rant before Police Judge Conlan for
the arrest of Maurice Donahue on a
charge of felony embezzlement.
Donahue is the absconding trustee
and so far no trace of him has been
found. It appears that during a
strike at one of the leading restau
rants in the early part of last month
members of the union became uneasy,
as they were threatened with suits
for damages, writs of injunction and
other legal procedure. A meeting
was held and it was decided to with
draw the funds amounting to $1230
from the treasurer's hands and the
bank and deposit them in the vaults
of the Columbian Banking, Company.
Trustee Donahue was ' entrusted
with the task and he withdrew the
money from bank on December 14.
He disappeared about that time and
when inquiry was made it was dis
covered that Donahue had deposited
the money in the vaults in the name
of R. S. Roberts and , had obtained
the key to the box. When the box
was opened with a duplicate key it
was found to be empty.
A call was made by an anxious del
egation at 34 Hawthorne street, where
Donahue lived, and it was learned
that on the night of December 14
Donahue packed his grip, saying he
was going on a short trip, and that
was the last seen or heard of him.
The police now have charge of the
case and every effort will be mad* to
locate the missing trustee.
MONNIER FAMILY TROUBLES
ARE DENIED BY THE WIDOW
Says She Did Not Call George Bad
Names and Denies Other Evi
dence Submitted.
A further cross-examination of Mrs.
Anna Monnier and more dirept exam
ination by her attorney kept the wid
ow of the wine d#aler on the stand
nearly all of yesterday, and the onl#
other witness was Miss Esther Har
din, who visited the Monnier home on
occasions. She declared that George
Monnier Jr. used bad language to
ward his father and mother, and she
told of an instance when, the young
man having either lost or pawned his
overcoat, the father became so indig
nant that he proclaimed that he
would never leave the* boy anything
in his will.
Mrs. Monnier was questiohed at
length regarding the many stories of
trouble in the family and' of the
father's attitude toward the boy. She
denied a great deal of the evidence
given by George and his wife and was
particularly emphatic in saying that
ihe had not called him an unmen~
tionable name. "If he says I did he's
a liar," said the angry witness.
Mr. Sullivan asked her about a will
made by Monnier in 1896, in which, it
was rumored, half of his fortune had
been left to the adopted son. She
said that her husband had given her
the will to read, but she had not
enough curiosity to peruse it. It had
been laid away in the safe and was
not destroyed until 1901, a long time
after the second will, in which George
was disinherited, was made.
